Megillah



Daf 1a



Daf 1b



Daf 2a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>The Megilla is read on the eleventh, on the twelfth, on the thirteenth, on the fourteenth,</b> or <b>on the fifteenth</b> of the month of Adar, <b>not earlier and not later.</b> The mishna explains the circumstances when the Megilla is read on each of these days. <b>Cities [<i>kerakin</i>] that have been surrounded by a wall since the days of Joshua, son of Nun, read</b> the Megilla <b>on the fifteenth</b> of Adar, whereas <b>villages and large towns</b> that have not been walled since the days of Joshua, son of Nun, <b>read</b> it <b>on the fourteenth. However,</b> the Sages instituted <b>that the villages may advance</b> their reading <b>to the day of assembly,</b> i.e., Monday or Thursday, when the rabbinical courts are in session and the Torah is read publicly, and the villagers therefore come to the larger towns.
<b>How so?</b> If <b>the fourteenth</b> of Adar <b>occurs on Monday, the villages and large towns read</b> it <b>on that day, and the walled cities</b> read it <b>on the next day,</b> the fifteenth. If the fourteenth <b>occurs on Tuesday or Wednesday, the villages advance</b> their reading <b>to the day of assembly,</b> i.e., Monday, the twelfth or thirteenth of Adar; <b>the large towns read</b> it <b>on that day,</b> i.e., the fourteenth of Adar, <b>and the walled cities</b> read it <b>on the next day,</b> the fifteenth.
If the fourteenth <b>occurs on Thursday, the villages and large towns read</b> it <b>on that day,</b> the fourteenth, <b>and the walled cities</b> read it <b>on the next day,</b> the fifteenth. If the fourteenth <b>occurs on Shabbat eve, the villages advance</b> their reading <b>to the day of assembly,</b> i.e., Thursday, the thirteenth of Adar; <b>and the large towns and the walled cities read</b> it <b>on that day,</b> i.e., the fourteenth of Adar. Even the walled cities read the Megilla on the fourteenth rather than on the fifteenth, as they do not read it on Shabbat.
If the fourteenth <b>occurs on Shabbat,</b> both <b>the villages and large towns advance</b> their reading <b>to the day of assembly,</b> i.e., Thursday, the twelfth of Adar; <b>and the walled cities</b> read it <b>on the day after</b> Purim, the fifteenth. If the fourteenth <b>occurs on Sunday, the villages advance</b> their reading <b>to the day of assembly,</b> i.e., Thursday, the eleventh of Adar; <b>and the large towns read</b> it <b>on that day,</b> i.e., the fourteenth of Adar; <b>and the walled cities</b> read it <b>on the next day,</b> the fifteenth.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> We learned in the mishna: <b>The Megilla is read on the eleventh</b> of Adar. The Gemara asks: <b>From where do we</b> derive this <i>halakha</i>? The Gemara expresses surprise at the question: What room is there to ask: <b>From where do we</b> derive this <i>halakha</i>? The reason is <b>as we intend to say further on: The Sages were lenient with the villages</b> and allowed them <b>to advance</b> their reading of the Megilla <b>to the day of assembly, so that they</b> would be free to <b>supply water and food to their brethren in the cities</b> on the day of Purim. Accordingly, the Megilla is read on the eleventh due to a rabbinic enactment.
The Gemara explains: <b>This is what we said,</b> i.e., this is what we meant when we asked the question: <b>Now, all of these</b> days when the Megilla may be read <b>were enacted by the members of the Great Assembly</b> when they established the holiday of Purim itself. <b>As, if it enters your mind</b> to say <b>that the members of the Great Assembly enacted</b> only <b>the fourteenth and fifteenth</b> as days for reading the Megilla, is it possible that <b>the</b> later <b>Sages came and uprooted an ordinance that was enacted by the members of the Great Assembly? Didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Eduyyot</i> 1:5) that <b>a rabbinical court cannot rescind the statements of another rabbinical court, unless it is superior to it in wisdom and in number?</b> No subsequent court was ever greater than the members of the Great Assembly, so it would be impossible for another court to rescind the enactments of the members of the Great Assembly.
<b>Rather, it is obvious</b> that <b>all</b> these days <b>were enacted by the members of the Great Assembly,</b> and the question is: <b>Where is the allusion</b> to this in the Bible? The Megilla itself, which was approved by the members of the Great Assembly, mentions only the fourteenth and fifteenth of Adar.
<b>Rav Shemen bar Abba said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> It is alluded to when <b>the verse states: “To confirm these days of Purim in their times”</b> (Esther 9:31). The phrase “in their times” indicates that <b>they enacted many times for them</b> and not only two days.
The Gemara objects: <b>This</b> verse <b>is necessary for its own</b> purpose, to teach that the days of Purim must be observed at the proper times. The Gemara responds: <b>If so, let the verse say:</b> To confirm these days of Purim in its <b>time. What is</b> the significance of the term <b>“their times,”</b> in the plural? It indicates that <b>many times</b> were established for the reading of the Megilla.
The Gemara objects: <b>But still,</b> the plural term is <b>necessary</b> to indicate that <b>the time of this</b> walled city <b>is not</b> the same as <b>the time of that</b> unwalled town, i.e., Purim is celebrated on different days in different places. The Gemara answers: <b>If so, let the verse say: Their time,</b> indicating that each place celebrates Purim on its respective day. <b>What</b> is the significance of the compound plural <b>“their times”? Learn from this</b> term that although the verse (Esther 9:21) specifies only two days, the Megilla may, at times, be read on <b>all of</b> the days enumerated in the mishna.
The Gemara asks: If so, <b>say</b> that the plural term indicates <b>many times,</b> and the Megilla may be read even earlier than the eleventh of Adar. The Gemara rejects this argument: The compound plural <b>“their times,”</b> should be understood as <b>similar to</b> the simple plural term, <b>their time. Just as</b> the term <b>their time</b> can be understood to refer to <b>two</b> days, indicating that each location reads the Megilla in its respective time on the fourteenth or the fifteenth of Adar, <b>so too, “their times”</b> should be understood as referring to only <b>two</b> additional days when the Megilla may be read.
The Gemara asks: <b>Say</b> that these two added days are <b>the twelfth and the thirteenth</b> of Adar. How is it derived that the Megilla may be read on the eleventh as well? The Gemara answers: It is <b>as Rav Shmuel bar Yitzḥak said</b> in a different context: <b>The thirteenth</b> of Adar <b>is a time of assembly for all,</b> as it was on that day that the Jews assembled to fight their enemies, and the main miracle was performed on that day. Consequently, <b>there is no need</b> for a special derivation <b>to include it</b> as a day that is fit for reading the Megilla. <b>Here too,</b> since <b>the thirteenth</b> of Adar <b>is a time of assembly for all, there is no need</b> for a special derivation <b>to include it</b> among the days when the Megilla may be read.
The Gemara objects: <b>And say</b> that the two additional days are <b>the sixteenth and the seventeenth</b> of Adar. The Gemara responds: <b>It is written: “And it shall not pass”</b> (Esther 9:27), indicating that the celebration of Purim is not delayed until a later date.
Having cited and discussed the opinion of Rav Shemen bar Abba, the Gemara cites another answer to the question of where the verses allude to the permissibility of reading the Megilla on the days enumerated in the mishna. <b>And Rabbi Shmuel bar Naḥmani said:</b> These dates are alluded to when <b>the verse states: “As the days on which the Jews rested from their enemies”</b> (Esther 9:22). The term <b>“days”</b> is referring to the two days that are explicitly mentioned in the previous verse, i.e., the fourteenth and the fifteenth. The term <b>“as the days”</b> comes <b>to include</b> two additional days, i.e., <b>the eleventh and twelfth</b> of Adar.
The Gemara asks: <b>And say</b> that the two additional days are <b>the twelfth and thirteenth</b> of Adar. How is it derived that the Megilla may be read on the eleventh as well? In answer to this question, <b>Rav Shmuel bar Yitzḥak said: The thirteenth</b> of Adar <b>is a time of assembly for all, and there is no need</b> for a special derivation <b>to include it</b> as a day fit for reading. The Gemara objects: <b>Say</b> that these additional days are <b>the sixteenth and seventeenth</b> of Adar. This suggestion is rejected: <b>It is written: “And it shall not pass.”</b>
Since two derivations were offered for the same matter, the Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason</b> that <b>Rabbi Shmuel bar Naḥmani did not state</b> that the days enumerated in the mishna are fit for reading the Megilla <b>based upon</b> the term <b>“in their times,”</b> in accordance with the opinion of Rav Shemen bar Abba? The Gemara answers: <b>He does not learn</b> anything from the distinction between the terms <b>time, their time,</b> and <b>their times.</b> Therefore, the verse indicates only that there are two days when the Megilla may be read.
The Gemara asks: <b>And what is the reason</b> that <b>Rav Shemen bar Abba did not state</b> that the days enumerated in the mishna are fit for reading the Megilla <b>based upon</b> the term <b>“as the days,”</b> in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Shmuel bar Naḥmani? The Gemara answers: He could have <b>said to you: That</b> verse <b>is written</b> as a reference <b>to</b> future <b>generations,</b> and it indicates that just as the Jews rested on these days at that time, they shall rest and celebrate on these days for all generations.
§ With regard to the mishna’s ruling that the Megilla may be read on the day of assembly, <b>Rabba bar bar Ḥana said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: This is the statement of Rabbi Akiva the unattributed.</b> Most unattributed statements of <i>tanna’im</i> were formulated by Rabbi Akiva’s students and reflect his opinions. <b>As, he derives</b> <i>halakhot</i> based on the distinction that he draws between the terms <b>time, their time, and their times. However, the Sages say: One may read</b> the Megilla <b>only in its</b> designated <b>time,</b> i.e., the fourteenth of Adar.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> based upon the following <i>baraita</i>. <b>Rabbi Yehuda said: When</b> is one permitted to read the Megilla from the eleventh to the fifteenth of Adar? One may read on these dates <b>at a time when the years are established</b> properly <b>and the Jewish people dwell</b> securely <b>in their</b> own <b>land. However, nowadays, since</b> people <b>look</b> to the reading of the Megilla and use it to calculate when Passover begins, <b>one may read</b> the Megilla <b>only in its</b> designated <b>time,</b> so as not to cause confusion with regard to the date of Passover, which is exactly one month from the day after Purim.
The Gemara analyzes this <i>baraita</i>: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion did <b>Rabbi Yehuda</b> issue his ruling? <b>If we say that it is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva,</b> whose opinion is expressed in the mishna, there is a difficulty, as Rabbi Akiva holds that <b>even nowadays this ordinance applies.</b> According to Rabbi Akiva, it is permitted for residents of villages to read the Megilla on the day of assembly even nowadays, as he did not limit his ruling to times when the Jewish people dwell securely in their land.
<b>Rather, is it not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the Sages,</b> who disagreed with Rabbi Akiva? <b>And,</b> nevertheless, <b>at least when the years are established</b> properly <b>and the Jewish people dwell</b> securely <b>in their land,</b> the Megilla <b>is read</b> even prior to the fourteenth, as the Sages disagree only about the <i>halakha</i> nowadays. This contradicts the statement of Rabbi Yoḥanan, who holds that the Megilla could never be read earlier than the fourteenth of Adar. The Gemara concludes: <b>The refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan is</b> indeed <b>a conclusive refutation.</b>
<b>There are</b> those <b>who say</b> a different version of the previous passage. <b>Rabba bar bar Ḥana said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: This is the statement of Rabbi Akiva, the unattributed. However, the Sages said: Nowadays, since</b> people <b>look</b> to the reading of the Megilla and use it to calculate when Passover begins, <b>one may read</b> the Megilla <b>only in its</b> designated <b>time.</b>
The Gemara comments: <b>This is also taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Yehuda said: When</b> is one permitted to read the Megilla from the eleventh to the fifteenth of Adar? <b>At a time when the years are established</b> properly <b>and the Jewish people dwell</b> securely <b>in their</b> own <b>land. However, nowadays, since</b> people <b>look</b> to the reading of the Megilla and use it to calculate when Passover begins, <b>one may read</b> the Megilla <b>only in its</b> designated <b>time.</b> According to this version, Rabbi Yehuda’s statement is consistent with the opinion of the Sages, as cited by Rabbi Yoḥanan.
The Gemara adds: <b>Rav Ashi</b> poses <b>a difficulty</b> based on an apparent contradiction between the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda</b> in the aforementioned <i>baraita</i> and a ruling cited in a mishna in the name of <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b>

Daf 2b

<b>and he establishes the <i>baraita</i> in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Yehuda,</b> rather than Rabbi Yehuda.
The Gemara explains the apparent contradiction: <b>And did Rabbi Yehuda</b> actually <b>say</b> that <b>nowadays, since</b> people <b>look</b> to the reading of the Megilla and use it to calculate when Passover begins, <b>one may read</b> the Megilla <b>only in its</b> designated <b>time?</b> The Gemara <b>raises a contradiction</b> from a mishna (5a): <b>Rabbi Yehuda said: When</b> is one permitted to read the Megilla from the eleventh of Adar? In <b>a place where</b> the villagers generally <b>enter</b> town <b>on Monday and Thursday. However,</b> in <b>a place where they do not</b> generally <b>enter</b> town <b>on Monday and Thursday, one may read</b> the Megilla <b>only in its</b> designated <b>time,</b> the fourteenth of Adar.
The mishna indicates that, <b>at least</b> in <b>a place where</b> the villagers <b>enter</b> town <b>on Monday and Thursday, one may read</b> the Megilla from the eleventh of Adar <b>even nowadays. And</b> due to this contradiction, Rav Ashi <b>establishes the <i>baraita</i> in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Yehuda.</b>
The Gemara expresses surprise: <b>Because</b> Rav Ashi poses <b>a difficulty</b> due to the apparent contradiction between the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda</b> in the <i>baraita</i> and the opinion cited in a mishna in the name of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, he establishes the <i>baraita</i> in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Yehuda?</b> How could he have emended the text just because he had a difficulty that he did not know how to resolve?
The Gemara explains: <b>Rav Ashi heard that there were those who taught</b> the <i>baraita</i> in the name of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, and there were those who taught it</b> in the name of <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Yehuda. And since he had a difficulty</b> with the apparent contradiction between one ruling of <b>Rabbi Yehuda and</b> another ruling of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, he said: The one who taught it</b> in the name of <b>Rabbi Yehuda is not precise,</b> whereas <b>the one who taught it</b> in the name of <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Yehuda is precise,</b> and in this way he eliminated the contradiction.
§ We learned in the mishna: <b>Cities that have been surrounded by a wall since the days of Joshua, son of Nun, read</b> the Megilla <b>on the fifteenth</b> of Adar. The Gemara asks: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived, as they are not stated explicitly in the Megilla? <b>Rava said:</b> It is <b>as the verse states: “Therefore the Jews of the villages, who dwell in the unwalled towns,</b> make the fourteenth day of the month of Adar a day of gladness and feasting” (Esther 9:19). <b>From</b> the fact that <b>the unwalled towns</b> celebrate Purim <b>on the fourteenth,</b> it may be derived that <b>the walled cities</b> celebrate Purim <b>on the fifteenth.</b>
The Gemara challenges this answer: <b>Say</b> that the <b>unwalled towns</b> celebrate Purim <b>on the fourteenth,</b> as indicated in the verse, and <b>the walled cities</b> do <b>not</b> celebrate it <b>at all.</b> The Gemara expresses astonishment: <b>And are they not Jews? And furthermore: It is written</b> that the kingdom of Ahasuerus was <b>“from Hodu until Cush”</b> (Esther 1:1), and the celebration of Purim was accepted in all of the countries of his kingdom (Esther 9:20–23).
Rather, the following challenge may be raised: <b>Say</b> that <b>the unwalled towns</b> celebrate Purim <b>on the fourteenth</b> and <b>the walled cities</b> celebrate it <b>on the fourteenth and on the fifteenth, as it is written: “That they should keep the fourteenth day of the month of Adar and the fifteenth day of the same, in every year”</b> (Esther 9:21). This verse can be understood to mean that there are places where Purim is celebrated on both days.
The Gemara rejects this argument: <b>If it had been written</b> in the verse: <b>The fourteenth day and [<i>ve</i>] the fifteenth,</b> it would be <b>as you</b> originally <b>said.</b> However, <b>now that it is written: The fourteenth day and [<i>ve’et</i>] the fifteenth day,</b> the particle <b><i>et</i></b> used here to denote the accusative <b>comes and interrupts,</b> indicating that the two days are distinct. Therefore, residents of <b>these</b> locations celebrate Purim <b>on the fourteenth, and</b> residents of <b>those</b> locations celebrate it <b>on the fifteenth.</b>
The Gemara suggests: <b>Say</b> that residents of <b>unwalled towns</b> celebrate Purim <b>on the fourteenth,</b> as stated in the verse, and with regard to residents of <b>walled cities, if they wish</b> they may celebrate it <b>on the fourteenth,</b> and <b>if they wish</b> they may celebrate it <b>on the fifteenth.</b> The Gemara responds: <b>The verse states: “In their times”</b> (Esther 9:31), indicating that <b>the time</b> when the residents of <b>this</b> place celebrate Purim <b>is not the time</b> when the residents of <b>that</b> place celebrate Purim.
The Gemara raises another challenge: <b>Say</b> that the walled cities should celebrate Purim <b>on the thirteenth</b> of Adar and not on the fifteenth. The Gemara answers: It stands to reason that the residents of walled cities, who do not celebrate Purim on the fourteenth, celebrate it <b>as</b> it is celebrated in <b>Shushan,</b> and it is explicitly stated that Purim was celebrated in Shushan on the fifteenth.
The Gemara comments: <b>We found</b> a source for <b>observing</b> the holiday of Purim on the fourteenth of Adar in unwalled towns and on the fifteenth of Adar in walled cities; <b>from where do we</b> derive that <b>remembering</b> the story of Purim through the reading of the Megilla takes place on these days? The Gemara explains that <b>the verse states: “That these days should be remembered and observed”</b> (Esther 9:28), from which it is derived that <b>remembering is compared to observing.</b>
§ The Gemara notes that <b>the mishna is not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>this <i>tanna</i>, as it is taught</b> in the <i>Tosefta</i> (1:1) that <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Korḥa says: Cities that have been surrounded by a wall since the days of Ahasuerus read</b> the Megilla <b>on the fifteenth.</b> According to the <i>Tosefta</i>, the status of walled cities is determined based upon whether they were walled in the time of Ahasuerus rather than the time of Joshua.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason for</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Korḥa?</b> The Gemara explains that the Megilla is read on the fifteenth in cities that are <b>like Shushan: Just as Shushan</b> is a city <b>that was surrounded by a wall since the days of Ahasuerus, and one reads</b> the Megilla there <b>on the fifteenth, so too every</b> city <b>that was walled since the days of Ahasuerus reads</b> the Megilla <b>on the fifteenth.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason for</b> the opinion of <b>the <i>tanna</i> of our</b> mishna? The Gemara explains: It is <b>derived</b> through a verbal analogy between one instance of the word <b>unwalled and</b> another instance of the word <b>unwalled. It is written here: “Therefore the Jews of the villages,</b> who dwell in the unwalled towns” (Esther 9:19), <b>and it is written there,</b> in Moses’ statement to Joshua before the Jewish people entered Eretz Yisrael: “All these cities were fortified with high walls, gates and bars; <b>besides unwalled towns, a great many”</b> (Deuteronomy 3:5). <b>Just as there,</b> in Deuteronomy, the reference is to a city <b>that was surrounded by a wall from the days of Joshua, son of Nun, so too here</b> it is referring to a city <b>that was surrounded by a wall from the days of Joshua, son of Nun.</b>
The Gemara continues: <b>Granted</b> that <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Korḥa did not state</b> his explanation <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the <i>tanna</i> of our</b> mishna <b>because he did not hold</b> that a verbal analogy can be established between one verse that employs the word <b>unwalled</b> and the other verse that employs the word <b>unwalled. However, what is the reason</b> that <b>the <i>tanna</i></b> of <b>our</b> mishna <b>did not state</b> his explanation <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Korḥa?</b>
The Gemara expresses astonishment: <b>What is the reason? Isn’t it</b> because <b>he holds</b> that it is derived from the verbal analogy between one usage of the word <b>unwalled</b> and the other usage of the word <b>unwalled?</b> The Gemara explains: <b>This is what he said,</b> i.e., this was the question: According to the <i>tanna</i> of our mishna, <b>in accordance with whom does Shushan observe</b> Purim? Shushan is <b>not like the unwalled towns and not like the walled cities,</b> as residents of Shushan celebrate Purim on the fifteenth, but the city was not surrounded by a wall since the days of Joshua.
<b>Rava said, and some say it unattributed</b> to any particular Sage: <b>Shushan is different since the miracle occurred in it</b> on the fifteenth of Adar, and therefore Purim is celebrated on that day. However, other cities are only considered walled cities and read the Megilla on the fifteenth of Adar if they were walled since the days of Joshua.
The Gemara asks: <b>Granted, according</b> to <b>the <i>tanna</i> of our</b> mishna, <b>this is</b> the meaning of <b>what is written:</b> “And these days should be remembered and observed throughout every generation, every family, <b>every province, and every city”</b> (Esther 9:28). The phrase <b>“every province [<i>medina</i>]”</b> is expressed in the verse using repetition, so that it reads literally: Every province and province, and therefore contains a superfluous usage of the word province, is meant <b>to distinguish between</b> cities <b>that were surrounded by a wall since the days of Joshua, son of Nun,</b> where the Megilla is read on the fifteenth, <b>and</b> a city that <b>was surrounded by a wall since the days of Ahasuerus,</b> where the Megilla is read on the fourteenth.
The phrase <b>“every city,”</b> which is similarly expressed through repetition and contains a superfluous usage of the word city, <b>also</b> serves <b>to distinguish between Shushan and other cities,</b> as Purim is celebrated in Shushan on the fifteenth despite the fact that it was not walled since the time of Joshua. <b>However, according to Rabbi Yehoshua ben Korḥa, granted</b> that the phrase <b>“every province”</b> comes <b>to distinguish between Shushan and other cities</b> that were not walled since the days of Ahasuerus; <b>but what does</b> the phrase <b>“every city” come</b> to teach?
The Gemara explains that <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Korḥa</b> could have <b>said to you: According to the <i>tanna</i> of our</b> mishna, <b>does it work out well? Since he holds</b> that it is derived from the verbal analogy between one verse that employs the word <b>unwalled</b> and the other verse that employs the word <b>unwalled, why do I</b> need the phrase <b>“every province”? Rather, the verse comes for</b> a midrashic <b>exposition, and it comes</b> to indicate that the <i>halakha</i> is <b>in accordance with</b> the ruling issued by <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi. As Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi said: A</b> walled <b>city, and all</b> settlements <b>adjacent to it, and all</b> settlements that can be <b>seen with it,</b> i.e., that can be seen from the walled city, are <b>considered like the</b> walled <b>city,</b> and the Megilla is read there on the fifteenth.
The Gemara asks: <b>Up to what</b> distance is considered adjacent? <b>Rabbi Yirmeya said, and some say</b> that it was <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba</b> who said: The limit is <b>like</b> the distance <b>from</b> the town of <b>Ḥamtan to Tiberias,</b> a <b><i>mil</i>.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Let him say</b> simply that the limit is a <b><i>mil</i>;</b> why did he have to mention these places? The Gemara answers that the formulation of the answer <b>teaches us this: How much</b> distance comprises <b>the measure</b> of a <b><i>mil</i>?</b> It is <b>like</b> the distance <b>from Ḥamtan to Tiberias.</b>
§ Having cited a statement of Rabbi Yirmeya, which some attribute to Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba, the Gemara cites other statements attributed to these Sages. <b>Rabbi Yirmeya said, and some say</b> that it was <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba</b> who said: <b>The Seers,</b> i.e., the prophets, were the ones who <b>said</b> that the letters <b><i>mem</i>, <i>nun</i>, <i>tzadi</i>, <i>peh</i>,</b> and <b><i>kaf</i></b> [<b><i>mantzepakh</i></b>], have a different form when they appear at the end of a word.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> how can <b>you understand</b> it that way? <b>Isn’t it written: “These are the commandments</b> that the Lord commanded Moses for the children of Israel in Mount Sinai” (Leviticus 27:34), which indicates <b>that a prophet is not permitted to initiate</b> or change <b>any matter</b> of <i>halakha</i> <b>from now on?</b> Consequently, how could the prophets establish new forms for the letters? <b>And furthermore, didn’t Rav Ḥisda say:</b> The letters <b><i>mem</i> and <i>samekh</i> in the tablets</b> of the covenant given at Sinai

Daf 3a

<b>stood by way of a miracle?</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>Yes,</b> two forms of these letters <b>did exist</b> at that time, <b>but</b> the people <b>did not know which</b> one of them was to be used <b>in the middle of the word and which at the end of the word, and the Seers came and established</b> that <b>the open</b> forms are to used be <b>in the middle of the word and the closed</b> forms <b>at the end of the word.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Ultimately,</b> however, doesn’t the phrase <b>“these are the commandments”</b> (Leviticus 27:34) indicate <b>that a prophet is not permitted to initiate any matter</b> of <i>halakha</i> <b>from now on? Rather,</b> it may be suggested that the final letters already existed at the time of the giving of the Torah, but over the course of time the people <b>forgot them,</b> and the prophets <b>then</b> came and <b>reestablished them.</b>
§ The Gemara cites another ruling of Rabbi Yirmeya or Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba. <b>Rabbi Yirmeya said, and some say</b> that it was <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba</b> who said: <b>The</b> Aramaic <b>translation of the Torah</b> used in the synagogues <b>was composed by Onkelos the convert based on</b> the teachings of <b>Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua. The</b> Aramaic <b>translation of the Prophets was composed by Yonatan ben Uzziel based on</b> a tradition going back to the last prophets, <b>Haggai, Zechariah, and Malachi.</b> The Gemara relates that when Yonatan ben Uzziel wrote his translation, <b>Eretz Yisrael quaked</b> over an area of <b>four hundred parasangs [<i>parsa</i>] by four hundred parasangs,</b> and <b>a Divine Voice emerged and said: Who is this who has revealed My secrets to mankind?</b>
<b>Yonatan ben Uzziel stood up on his feet and said: I am the one who has revealed Your secrets to mankind</b> through my translation. However, <b>it is revealed and known to You that I did this not for my</b> own <b>honor, and not for the honor of the house of</b> my <b>father, but rather</b> it was <b>for Your honor</b> that <b>I did this, so that discord not increase among the Jewish people.</b> In the absence of an accepted translation, people will disagree about the meaning of obscure verses, but with a translation, the meaning will be clear.
<b>And</b> Yonatan ben Uzziel <b>also sought to reveal a translation of the Writings,</b> but <b>a Divine Voice emerged and said to him: It is enough for you</b> that you translated the Prophets. The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reason</b> that he was denied permission to translate the Writings? <b>Because it has in it</b> a revelation of <b>the end,</b> when the <b>Messiah</b> will arrive. The end is foretold in a cryptic manner in the book of Daniel, and were the book of Daniel translated, the end would become manifestly revealed to all.
The Gemara asks: <b>Was the translation of the Torah</b> really <b>composed by Onkelos the convert? Didn’t Rav Ika bar Avin say</b> that <b>Rav Ḥananel said</b> that <b>Rav said: What is</b> the meaning of that <b>which is written</b> with respect to the days of Ezra: <b>“And they read in the book, the Torah of God, distinctly; and they gave the sense, and they caused them to understand the reading”</b> (Nehemiah 8:8)? The verse should be understood as follows: <b>“And they read in the book, the Torah of God,” this is the</b> scriptural <b>text; “distinctly,” this is the translation,</b> indicating that they immediately translated the text into Aramaic, as was customary during public Torah readings.
<b>“And they gave the sense,” these are</b> the divisions of the text into separate <b>verses. “And they caused them to understand the reading,” these are the cantillation notes,</b> through which the meaning of the text is further clarified. <b>And some say</b> that <b>these are the</b> Masoretic <b>traditions</b> with regard to the manner in which each word is to be written. This indicates that the Aramaic translation already existed at the beginning of the Second Temple period, well before the time of Onkelos. The Gemara answers: <b>The</b> ancient Aramaic translation <b>was forgotten and then</b> Onkelos came and <b>reestablished it.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What is different</b> about the translation of Prophets? Why is it that when Onkelos revealed the translation <b>of the Torah,</b> Eretz Yisrael <b>did not quake, and</b> when he revealed the translation <b>of the Prophets, it quaked?</b> The Gemara explains: <b>The</b> meaning of <b>matters</b> discussed <b>in the Torah is clear,</b> and therefore its Aramaic translation did not reveal the meaning of passages that had not been understood previously. Conversely, in <b>the Prophets, there are matters that are clear and there are matters that are obscure,</b> and the Aramaic translation revealed the meaning of obscure passages. The Gemara cites an example of an obscure verse that is clarified by the Aramaic translation: <b>As it is written: “On that day shall there be a great mourning in Jerusalem, like the mourning of Hadadrimmon in the valley of Megiddon”</b> (Zechariah 12:11).
<b>And</b> with regard to that verse, <b>Rav Yosef said: Were it not for the</b> Aramaic <b>translation of this verse, we would not have known what it is saying,</b> as the Bible does not mention any incident involving Hadadrimmon in the valley of Megiddon. The Aramaic translation reads as follows: <b>On that day, the mourning in Jerusalem will be as great as the mourning for Ahab, son of Omri, who was slain by Hadadrimmon, son of Tavrimon, in Ramoth-Gilead, and like the mourning for Josiah, son of Amon, who was slain by Pharaoh the lame in the valley of Megiddon.</b> The translation clarifies that the verse is referring to two separate incidents of mourning, and thereby clarifies the meaning of this verse.
§ The Gemara introduces another statement from the same line of tradition. The verse states: <b>“And I, Daniel, alone saw the vision, for the men who were with me did not see the vision; but a great trembling fell upon them, so that they fled to hide themselves”</b> (Daniel 10:7). <b>Who were these men?</b> The term “men” in the Bible indicates important people; who were they? <b>Rabbi Yirmeya said, and some say</b> that it was <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba</b> who said: <b>These are</b> the prophets <b>Haggai, Zechariah, and Malachi.</b>
The Gemara comments: In certain ways <b>they,</b> the prophets, <b>were greater than him,</b> Daniel, and in certain ways <b>he,</b> Daniel, <b>was greater than them. They were greater than him, as they were prophets and he was not a prophet.</b> Haggai, Zechariah, and Malachi were sent to convey the word of God to the Jewish people, while Daniel was not sent to reveal his visions to others. In another way, however, <b>he was greater than them, as he saw</b> this vision, <b>and they did not see</b> this vision, indicating that his ability to perceive obscure and cryptic visions was greater than theirs.
The Gemara asks: <b>Since they did not see</b> the vision, <b>what is the reason that they were frightened?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Even though they did not see</b> the vision, <b>their guardian angels saw</b> it, and therefore they sensed that there was something fearful there and they fled.
<b>Ravina said: Learn from</b> this incident that with regard to <b>one who is frightened</b> for no apparent reason, <b>although he does not see</b> anything menacing, <b>his guardian angel sees</b> it, and therefore he should take steps in order to escape the danger. The Gemara asks: <b>What is his remedy? He should recite <i>Shema</i>,</b> which will afford him protection. <b>And if he is standing in a place of filth,</b> where it is prohibited to recite verses from the Torah, <b>he should distance himself four cubits from his</b> current <b>location</b> in order to escape the danger. <b>And if</b> he is <b>not</b> able to do so, <b>let him say the following</b> incantation: <b>The goat of the slaughterhouse is fatter than I am,</b> and if a calamity must fall upon something, it should fall upon it.
§ After this digression, the Gemara returns to the exposition of a verse cited above. <b>Now</b> that <b>you have said</b> that the phrases <b>“every province” and “every city”</b> appear <b>for</b> the purposes of midrashic <b>exposition, for what</b> exposition do the words <b>“every family”</b> appear in that same verse (Esther 9:28)? <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Ḥanina said:</b> These words come <b>to include the priestly and Levitical families,</b> and indicate <b>that they cancel their service</b> in the Temple <b>and come to hear the reading of the Megilla.</b>
<b>As Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Rav said: The priests at their</b> Temple <b>service, the Levites on their platform</b> in the Temple, where they sung the daily psalm, <b>and the Israelites at their watches,</b> i.e., the group of Israelites, corresponding to the priestly watches, who would come to Jerusalem and gather in other locations as representatives of the entire nation to observe or pray for the success of the Temple service, <b>all cancel their service and come to hear the reading of the Megilla.</b>
<b>This is also taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>The priests at their service, the Levites on the platform, and the Israelites at their watches, all cancel their service and come to hear the reading of the Megilla.</b> The Sages of <b>the house of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>relied</b> upon the <i>halakha</i> stated <b>here</b> and determined <b>that one cancels his Torah study and comes to hear the reading of the Megilla.</b> They derived this principle by means of <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference <b>from the</b> Temple <b>service: Just as</b> one who is engaged in performing <b>service</b> in the Temple, <b>which is</b> very <b>important, cancels</b> his service in order to hear the Megilla, is it <b>not all the more so</b> obvious that one who is engaged in <b>Torah study</b> cancels his study to hear the Megilla?
The Gemara asks: <b>Is the</b> Temple <b>service more important than Torah study? Isn’t it written: “And it came to pass when Joshua was by Jericho that he lifted up his eyes and looked, and behold, a man stood over against him</b> with his sword drawn in his hand. And Joshua went over to him and said to him: Are you for us, or for our adversaries? And he said, No, but I am captain of the host of the Lord, I have come now. And Joshua fell on his face to the earth, <b>and bowed down”</b> (Joshua 5:13–14).
The Gemara first seeks to clarify the incident described in the verse. <b>How did</b> Joshua <b>do this,</b> i.e., how could he bow to a figure he did not recognize? <b>Didn’t Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi say: It is prohibited for a person to greet his fellow at night</b> if he does not recognize him, as <b>we are concerned that perhaps it is a demon?</b> How did Joshua know that it was not a demon? The Gemara answers: <b>There it was different, as</b> the visitor <b>said to him: But I am captain of the host of the Lord.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Perhaps</b> this was a demon <b>and he lied?</b> The Gemara answers: It <b>is learned</b> as a tradition that demons <b>do not utter the name of Heaven for naught,</b> and therefore since the visitor had mentioned the name of God, Joshua was certain that this was indeed an angel.
As for the angel’s mission, the Gemara explains that the angel <b>said to</b> Joshua: <b>Yesterday,</b> i.e., during the afternoon, <b>you neglected the afternoon daily offering</b> due to the impending battle, <b>and now,</b> at night, <b>you have neglected Torah study,</b> and I have come to rebuke you. Joshua <b>said to him: For which of these</b> sins <b>have you come? He said to him: I have come now,</b> indicating that neglecting Torah study is more severe than neglecting to sacrifice the daily offering. Joshua <b>immediately</b> determined to rectify the matter, as the verses states: <b>“And Joshua lodged that night”</b> (Joshua 8:9) <b>“in the midst of the valley</b> [<b><i>ha’emek</i></b>]” (Joshua 8:13), and <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b>

Daf 3b

This <b>teaches that he spent the night in the depths</b> [<b><i>be’umeka</i></b>] <b>of <i>halakha</i>,</b> i.e., that he spent the night studying Torah with the Jewish people. <b>And Rav Shmuel bar Unya said: Torah study is greater than sacrificing the daily offerings, as it is stated: “I have come now”</b> (Joshua 5:14), indicating that the angel came to rebuke Joshua for neglecting Torah study and not for neglecting the daily offering. Consequently, how did the Sages of the house of Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi determine that the Temple service is more important than Torah study?
The Gemara explains that it is <b>not difficult. This</b> statement, with regard to the story of Joshua, is referring to Torah study <b>by the masses,</b> which is greater than the Temple service. <b>That</b> statement of the Sages of the house of Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi is referring to Torah study <b>by an individual,</b> which is less significant than the Temple service.
The Gemara asks: Is the Torah study <b>of an individual a light</b> matter? <b>Didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna: <b>On</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival, women may lament</b> the demise of the deceased in unison, <b>but they may not clap</b> their hands in mourning? <b>Rabbi Yishmael says: Those that are close to the bier may clap. On the New Moon, on Hanukkah, and on Purim,</b> which are not mandated by Torah law, <b>they may</b> both <b>lament and clap</b> their hands in mourning. However, <b>on both</b> groups of days, <b>they may not wail</b> responsively, a form of wailing where one woman wails and the others repeat after her.
<b>And Rabba bar Huna said:</b> All these regulations were said with regard to an ordinary person, but <b>there are no</b> restrictions on expressions of mourning on the intermediate days of <b>a Festival in the presence of a</b> deceased <b>Torah scholar.</b> If a Torah scholar dies on the intermediate days of a Festival, the women may lament, clap, and wail responsively as on any other day, <b>and all the more so on Hanukkah and Purim.</b> This indicates that even the Torah study of an individual is of great importance.
The Gemara rejects this argument: <b>You speak of the honor</b> that must be shown <b>to the Torah,</b> and indeed, <b>the honor</b> that must be shown to <b>the Torah</b> in the case of <b>an individual</b> Torah scholar <b>is important;</b> but <b>the Torah study of an individual</b> in itself <b>is light</b> and is less significant than the Temple service.
§ <b>Rava said:</b> It <b>is obvious to me</b> that if one must choose between Temple <b>service and reading the Megilla, reading the Megilla</b> takes <b>precedence, based upon</b> the exposition of <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Ḥanina</b> with regard to the phrase “every family” (Esther 9:28). Similarly, if one must choose between <b>Torah study and reading the Megilla, reading the Megilla</b> takes <b>precedence, based upon</b> the fact that the Sages of <b>the house of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>relied</b> on Rabbi Yosei bar Ḥanina’s exposition to rule that one interrupts Torah study to hear the reading of the Megilla.
Furthermore, it is obvious that if one must choose between <b>Torah study and</b> tending to <b>a corpse with no one to bury it [<i>met mitzva</i>],</b> the task of burying <b>the <i>met mitzva</i></b> takes <b>precedence.</b> This is derived <b>from that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One cancels his Torah study to bring out a corpse</b> for burial, <b>and to</b> join a wedding procession and <b>bring in the bride.</b> Similarly, if one must choose between the Temple <b>service and</b> tending to <b>a <i>met mitzva</i>,</b> tending to the <b><i>met mitzva</i></b> takes <b>precedence, based upon</b> the <i>halakha</i> derived from the term <b>“or for his sister”</b> (Numbers 6:7).
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to verses addressing the laws of a nazirite: “All the days that he consecrates himself to the Lord, he shall not come near to a dead body. For his father, or for his mother, for his brother, or for his sister, he shall not make himself ritually impure for them when they die” (Numbers 6:6–7). <b>What</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states “or for his sister”?</b> The previous verse, which states that the nazirite may not come near a dead body, already prohibits him from becoming impure through contact with his sister. Therefore, the second verse is understood to be teaching a different <i>halakha</i>: One <b>who was going to slaughter his Paschal</b> lamb <b>or to circumcise his son, and he heard that</b> a relative <b>of his died,</b> one <b>might</b> have thought that <b>he</b> should return and <b>become ritually impure</b> with the impurity imparted by a corpse.
<b>You said: He shall not become impure;</b> the death of his relative will not override so significant a mitzva from the Torah. One <b>might</b> have thought: <b>Just as he does not become impure for his sister, so he does not become impure for a corpse with no one to bury it [<i>met mitzva</i>]. The verse states: “Or for his sister”; he may not become impure for his sister,</b> as someone else can attend to her burial, <b>but he does become impure for a <i>met mitzva</i>.</b>
On the basis of these premises, <b>Rava raised a dilemma:</b> If one must choose between <b>reading the Megilla and</b> tending to <b>a <i>met mitzva</i>, which of them</b> takes <b>precedence?</b> Does <b>reading the Megilla</b> take <b>precedence due to</b> the value of <b>publicizing the miracle, or perhaps</b> burying <b>the <i>met mitzva</i></b> takes <b>precedence due to</b> the value of preserving <b>human dignity? After he raised the dilemma,</b> Rava <b>then resolved it</b> on his own and ruled that attending to a <b><i>met mitzva</i></b> takes <b>precedence, as the Master said: Great is human dignity, as it overrides a prohibition in the Torah.</b> Consequently, it certainly overrides the duty to read the Megilla, despite the fact that reading the Megilla publicizes the miracle.
§ The Gemara examines <b>the</b> matter <b>itself</b> cited in the course of the previous discussion. <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi said: A</b> walled <b>city, and all</b> settlements <b>adjacent to it, and all</b> settlements that can be <b>seen with it,</b> i.e., that can be seen from the walled city, are <b>considered like the</b> walled <b>city,</b> and the Megilla is read on the fifteenth. It was <b>taught</b> in the <i>Tosefta</i>: This is the <i>halakha</i> with regard to a settlement <b>adjacent</b> to a walled city, <b>although it cannot be seen</b> from it, and also a place that <b>can be seen</b> from the walled city, <b>although it is not adjacent</b> to it.
The Gemara examines the <i>Tosefta</i>: <b>Granted</b> that with regard to a place that <b>can be seen</b> from the walled city, <b>although it is not adjacent</b> to it, <b>you find it where</b> the place <b>is located on the top of a mountain,</b> and therefore it can be seen from the walled city, although it is at some distance from it. <b>However,</b> with regard to a settlement that is <b>adjacent</b> to a walled city <b>although it cannot be seen</b> from it, <b>how can you find these</b> circumstances? <b>Rabbi Yirmeya said:</b> You find it, for example, <b>where</b> the place <b>is located in a valley,</b> and therefore it is possible that it cannot be seen from the walled city, although it is very close to it.
<b>And Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi said: A</b> walled <b>city that was</b> initially <b>settled and</b> only <b>later surrounded</b> by a wall <b>is considered a village</b> rather than a walled city. <b>What is the reason? As it is written: “And if a man sells a residential house in a walled city”</b> (Leviticus 25:29). The wording of the verse indicates that it is referring to a place <b>that was</b> first <b>surrounded</b> by a wall <b>and</b> only <b>later settled, and not</b> to a place <b>that was</b> first <b>settled and</b> only <b>later surrounded</b> by a wall.
<b>And Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi said: A</b> walled <b>city that does not have ten idlers,</b> i.e., individuals who do not work and are available to attend to communal needs, <b>is treated as a village.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What is he teaching us? We</b> already <b>learned</b> in a mishna (5a): <b>What is a large city? Any</b> city <b>in which there are ten idlers;</b> however, if there are <b>fewer than that, it is a village.</b> The Gemara answers: Nevertheless, <b>it was necessary</b> for Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi to teach this <i>halakha</i> with regard to a large <b>city,</b> to indicate that <b>even if</b> idlers <b>happen to come there from elsewhere,</b> since they are not local residents, it is still considered a village.
<b>And Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi</b> also <b>said: A</b> walled <b>city that was destroyed and</b> then <b>later settled is considered a city.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> meant by the term <b>destroyed? If we say</b> that the city’s <b>walls were destroyed,</b> and Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi comes to teach us that if it was <b>settled, yes</b> it is treated as a walled city, but if it was <b>not settled,</b> it is <b>not</b> treated that way, there is a difficulty. <b>Isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Eliezer bar Yosei says:</b> The verse states: <b>“Which has [<i>lo</i>] a wall</b> (Leviticus 25:30),” and the word <i>lo</i> is written with an <i>alef</i>, which means no, but in context the word <i>lo</i> is used as though it was written with a <i>vav</i>, meaning that it has a wall. This indicates that <b>even though</b> the city <b>does not have</b> a wall <b>now,</b> as the wall was destroyed, if it <b>had</b> a wall <b>before,</b> it retains its status as a walled city.
<b>Rather, what is</b> meant by the term <b>destroyed? That it was destroyed</b> in the sense that it no longer has <b>ten idlers,</b> and therefore it is treated like a village. However, once it has ten idlers again, it is treated like a city.
<b>And Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi said:</b>

Daf 4a

The cities <b>Lod, and Ono, and Gei HeḤarashim</b> are cities <b>that have been surrounded</b> by walls <b>since the days of Joshua, son of Nun.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Did Joshua</b>, son of Nun, really <b>build these</b> cities? <b>Didn’t Elpaal build them</b> at a later date, <b>as it is written: “And the sons of Elpaal: Eber, and Misham, and Shemed, who built Ono and Lod, with its hamlets”</b> (I Chronicles 8:12)? The Gemara counters: <b>According to your reasoning,</b> that this verse proves that these cities were built later, you can also say that <b>Asa,</b> king of Judah, <b>built them, as it is written: “And he,</b> Asa, <b>built fortified cities in Judah”</b> (see II Chronicles 14:5). Therefore, it is apparent that these cities were built more than once.
<b>Rabbi Elazar said: These</b> cities <b>were surrounded by a wall since the days of Joshua, son of Nun,</b> and they <b>were destroyed in the days of the concubine in Gibea,</b> as they stood in the tribal territory of Benjamin, and in that war all of the cities of Benjamin were destroyed (see Judges, chapters 19–21). <b>Elpaal</b> then <b>came and built them</b> again. <b>They then fell</b> in the wars between Judah and Israel, and <b>Asa came and restored them.</b>
The Gemara comments: The language of the verse <b>is also precise</b> according to this explanation, <b>as it is written </b>with regard to Asa: <b>“And he said to Judah: Let us build these cities”</b> (II Chronicles 14:6), which proves <b>by inference that they had</b> already <b>been cities at the outset,</b> and that he did not build new cities. The Gemara concludes: Indeed, <b>learn from this</b> that it is so.
§ <b>And Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi</b> also <b>said: Women are obligated in the reading of the Megilla, as they too were</b> significant partners <b>in that miracle. And Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi</b> also <b>said:</b> When <b>Purim occurs on Shabbat, one asks</b> questions <b>and expounds upon the subject of the day.</b>
The Gemara raises a question with regard to the last <i>halakha</i>: <b>Why</b> was it necessary to <b>specify Purim?</b> The same principle applies <b>also to the Festivals, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Moses enacted for the Jewish people that they should ask</b> questions about <b>and expound upon the subject of the day:</b> They should occupy themselves with <b>the <i>halakhot</i> of Passover on Passover,</b> with <b>the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>Shavuot</i> on <i>Shavuot</i>, and</b> with the <b><i>halakhot</i> of the festival</b> of <i>Sukkot</i> <b>on the festival</b> of <i>Sukkot</i>.
The Gemara answers: <b>It was necessary for</b> Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi to mention <b>Purim, lest you say</b> that when Purim falls on Shabbat <b>we should decree</b> that it is prohibited to expound upon the <i>halakhot</i> of the day <b>due to</b> the concern of <b>Rabba,</b> who said that the reason the Megilla is not read on a Purim that falls on Shabbat is due to a concern that one carry the Megilla in the public domain. Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi <b>therefore teaches us</b> that expounding the <i>halakhot</i> of the day is not prohibited as a preventive measure lest one read the Megilla on Shabbat.
<b>And Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi</b> further <b>said</b> with regard to Purim: <b>A person is obligated to read the Megilla at night and</b> then <b>to repeat it</b> [<b><i>lishnota</i></b>] <b>during the day, as it is stated: “O my God, I call by day but You do not answer; and at night, and there is no surcease for me”</b> (Psalms 22:3), which alludes to reading the Megilla both by day and by night.
Some of the students who heard this statement <b>understood from it</b> that one is obligated <b>to read</b> the Megilla <b>at night and to study its</b> relevant tractate of <b>Mishna by day,</b> as the term <i>lishnota</i> can be understood to mean studying Mishna. <b>Rabbi Yirmeya said to them: It was explained to me</b> personally <b>by Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba himself</b> that the term <i>lishnota</i> here has a different connotation, <b>for example, as people say: I will conclude this section and repeat it,</b> i.e., I will review my studies. Similarly, Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi’s statement means that one must repeat the reading of the Megilla by day after reading it at night.
The Gemara notes that this ruling <b>was also stated</b> by another <i>amora</i>, as <b>Rabbi Ḥelbo said</b> that <b>Ulla Bira’a said: A person is obligated to read the Megilla at night and</b> then <b>repeat it during the day, as it is stated: “So that my glory may sing praise to You and not be silent; O Lord, my God, I will give thanks to You forever”</b> (Psalms 30:13). The dual formulation of singing praise and not being silent alludes to reading the Megilla both by night and by day.
§ We learned in the mishna that residents of unwalled towns read the Megilla on the fourteenth of Adar; <b>however,</b> residents of <b>villages may advance</b> their reading <b>to the day of assembly,</b> the Monday or Thursday preceding Purim. <b>Rabbi Ḥanina said: The Sages were lenient with the villages</b> and allowed them <b>to advance</b> their reading of the Megilla <b>to the day of assembly, so that they</b> could be free to <b>provide water and food to their brethren in the cities</b> on the day of Purim. If everyone would be busy reading the Megilla on the fourteenth, the residents of the cities would not have enough to eat.

Daf 4b

The Gemara asks: <b>Is that to say that</b> this ordinance is <b>for the benefit of the cities? Didn’t we learn</b> in the mishna that if the fourteenth <b>occurred on a Monday, the</b> residents of <b>villages and large towns read it on that very day? If it is so,</b> that the ordinance allowing the villagers to sometimes advance their reading of the Megilla is for the benefit of the cities, <b>let</b> the villagers <b>advance</b> their reading <b>to the</b> previous <b>day of assembly</b> even when the fourteenth occurs on a Monday. The Gemara responds: That would mean that Megilla reading <b>for them</b> would take place on <b>the tenth</b> of Adar, <b>and the Sages did not establish the tenth</b> of Adar as a day that is fit to read the Megilla.
The Gemara continues: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof from a different statement of the mishna: If the fourteenth <b>occurs on a Thursday, the villages and large towns read</b> it <b>on that day,</b> the fourteenth, <b>and the walled cities</b> read it <b>on the next day,</b> the fifteenth. <b>If it is so,</b> that the ordinance is for the benefit of the cities, <b>let</b> the villagers <b>advance</b> their reading of the Megilla <b>to the</b> previous <b>day of assembly,</b> i.e., the previous Monday, as it is <b>the eleventh</b> of Adar. The Gemara responds: <b>We do not defer</b> the reading of the Megilla <b>from</b> one <b>day of assembly to</b> another <b>day of assembly.</b>
The Gemara continues: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> that which was taught in the following mishna (5a): <b>Rabbi Yehuda said: When</b> is the Megilla read from the eleventh of Adar and onward? <b>In a place where</b> the villagers generally <b>enter</b> town <b>on Monday and Thursday. However,</b> in a <b>place where they do not</b> generally <b>enter</b> town <b>on Monday and Thursday, one may read</b> the Megilla <b>only in its</b> designated <b>time,</b> the fourteenth of Adar. The Gemara infers: <b>If it enters your mind</b> to say that the ordinance is for <b>the benefit of the cities,</b> would it be reasonable to suggest that <b>because</b> the villagers <b>do not enter</b> town <b>on Monday and Thursday the</b> residents of the <b>cities</b> should <b>lose out</b> and not be provided with food and water?
The Gemara accepts this argument: <b>Do not say</b> that the Sages allowed the villages to advance their reading of the Megilla to the day of assembly <b>so that they</b> can be free to <b>provide water and food to their brethren in the cities</b> on the day of Purim. <b>Rather, say</b> that the Sages were lenient with them <b>because</b> the villages <b>supply water and food to their brethren in the cities.</b> This ordinance was established for the benefit of the villagers so that they should not have to make an extra trip to the cities to hear the reading of the Megilla. However, in a place where the villages do not go to the cities, advancing their reading of the Megilla to the day of assembly will not benefit them, and therefore they must read on the fourteenth.
§ We learned in the mishna: <b>How so?</b> If the fourteenth of Adar <b>occurs on Monday, the villages and large towns read</b> it <b>on that day.</b> The mishna continues to explain the days on which the Megilla is read. The Gemara asks: <b>What is different</b> about <b>the first clause</b> of the mishna, <b>which employs the order</b> of the dates <b>of the month,</b> i.e., the eleventh of Adar, <b>and the latter clause, which employs the order of the days</b> of the week, i.e., Monday?
The Gemara answers: <b>Since</b> the days of the week <b>would be reversed</b> if the latter clause was organized according to the dates of the month, as the mishna would first have to mention a case where the fourteenth occurs on a Sunday, then a case where it occurs on a Wednesday or Shabbat, and then a case where it occurs on a Friday or Tuesday, the mishna <b>employed the order of the days</b> of the week in order to avoid confusion.
§ We learned in the mishna: If the fourteenth <b>occurs on Shabbat eve,</b> Friday, the villages advance their reading to the day of assembly, i.e., Thursday, and the large towns and walled cities read it on Friday, the fourteenth of Adar. The Gemara asks: <b>Whose</b> opinion is expressed in the <b>mishna?</b> It can be <b>either Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>or Rabbi Yosei.</b>
The Gemara explains: <b>What is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi? <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If the fourteenth <b>occurs on Shabbat eve, villages and large towns advance</b> their reading <b>to the day of assembly,</b> i.e., Thursday, <b>and walled cities read it on the day</b> of Purim, Friday. <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi disagrees and <b>says: I say</b> that <b>the</b> readings in the large <b>towns should not be deferred from their</b> usual <b>date,</b> i.e., the fourteenth of Adar. <b>Rather, both these,</b> the large towns <b>and those,</b> the walled cities, <b>read</b> the Megilla <b>on the day</b> of Purim.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason of the first <i>tanna</i>?</b> The Gemara explains that it is <b>as it is written:</b> “To keep these two days, according to their writing and according to their time, <b>in every year”</b> (Esther 9:27), which indicates that Purim must be celebrated every year in similar fashion. <b>Just as</b> in <b>every</b> other <b>year</b> the large <b>towns precede the walled cities</b> by one day, <b>so too here the</b> large <b>towns precede the walled cities</b> by one day. Consequently, since the walled cities cannot read the Megilla on Shabbat and they are required to advance the reading to Friday, the large towns must also advance their reading a day to Thursday.
The Gemara raises an objection: <b>Say</b> that the words <b>“in every year”</b> indicate that <b>just as</b> in <b>every</b> other <b>year the</b> Megilla readings in <b>the</b> large <b>towns are not deferred from their</b> usual <b>date</b> and they read the Megilla on the fourteenth, <b>so too here the</b> Megilla readings in <b>the</b> large <b>towns should not be deferred from their</b> usual <b>date</b> and they too should read on the fourteenth. The Gemara answers: <b>Here it is different, as it is not possible</b> for the large towns to fulfill all of the conditions at the same time, i.e., to read on the fourteenth and to read a day before the walled cities.
The Gemara asks: <b>And Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, <b>what is</b> his <b>reason?</b> The Gemara explains that it is also based upon the words <b>“in every year”; just as</b> in <b>every</b> other <b>year the</b> readings in <b>the</b> large <b>towns are not deferred from their</b> usual <b>date</b> and they read on the fourteenth, <b>so too here, the</b> readings in <b>the</b> large <b>towns are not deferred from their</b> usual <b>date,</b> but rather they read on the fourteenth.
The Gemara raises an objection: <b>Say</b> that the words <b>“in every year”</b> indicate that <b>just as every year the</b> large <b>towns precede the walled cities</b> by one day, and read on the fourteenth, <b>so too here the</b> large <b>towns precede the walled cities</b> by one day, and read on the thirteenth. The Gemara answers: <b>Here it is different, as it is not possible</b> to fulfill all of the conditions at the same time, i.e., to read on the fourteenth and to read a day before the walled cities.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei? As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If the fourteenth <b>occurs on Shabbat eve, the walled cities and villages advance</b> their reading of the Megilla <b>to the day of assembly, and the large towns read</b> it <b>on the day</b> of Purim <b>itself. Rabbi Yosei says: The walled cities never precede the</b> large <b>towns; rather, both these,</b> the large towns, <b>and those,</b> the walled cities, <b>read on that day,</b> i.e., Friday, the fourteenth of Adar.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason of the first <i>tanna</i>? As it is written: “In every year”; just as</b> in <b>every</b> other <b>year the</b> large <b>towns</b> read the Megilla <b>on the fourteenth, and the time for this</b> type of settlement to read the Megilla <b>is not the time for that</b> type of settlement to read the Megilla, as the large towns and walled cities never read the Megilla on the same day, <b>so too here, the</b> large <b>towns</b> read the Megilla <b>on the fourteenth, and the time for this</b> type of settlement to read the Megilla <b>is not the time for that</b> type of settlement to read the Megilla. Therefore, the walled cities must advance their reading of the Megilla by two days to the day of assembly, Thursday.
The Gemara raises an objection: <b>Say</b> that the words <b>“in every year”</b> indicate that <b>just as</b> in <b>every</b> other <b>year the walled cities do not precede the</b> large <b>towns, so too here, the walled cities do not precede the</b> large <b>towns.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Here it is different, as it is not possible</b> to fulfill all of the conditions at the same time, i.e., that the large towns should read on the fourteenth, the large towns and the walled cities should read on different days, and the walled cities should not precede the large towns.
<b>What is the reason of Rabbi Yosei?</b> It is based upon the words <b>“in every year”; just as</b> in <b>every</b> other <b>year the walled cities do not precede the</b> large towns, <b>so too here, the walled cities do not precede the</b> large <b>towns.</b>
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>Say</b> that the words <b>“in every year” indicate</b> that <b>just as</b> in <b>every</b> other <b>year, the time for this</b> type of settlement to read the Megilla <b>is not the time for that</b> type of settlement to read the Megilla, <b>so too here, the time for this</b> type of settlement to read the Megilla <b>is not the time for that</b> type of settlement to read the Megilla. Therefore, since the large towns read on the fourteenth, the walled cities read on the thirteenth. The Gemara answers: <b>Here it is different, as it is not possible</b> to fulfill all the conditions. It is clear from these <i>baraitot</i> that the <i>tanna</i> of the mishna can either be Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi or Rabbi Yosei, but not either of two anonymous <i>tanna’im</i>.
The Gemara asks: <b>Does Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi really <b>hold that one does not defer</b> the reading of the Megilla in large towns <b>to the day of assembly? Isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If the fourteenth <b>occurs on Shabbat, the villages advance</b> their reading of the Megilla <b>to the day of assembly, the large towns read</b> it <b>on Shabbat eve, and the walled cities</b> read it <b>the next day,</b> i.e., on Sunday. <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says: I say</b> that <b>since the</b> readings in <b>the</b> large <b>towns were</b> already <b>deferred from their</b> usual <b>date,</b> i.e., the fourteenth, <b>they are deferred to the day of assembly,</b> i.e., to Thursday. Consequently, even Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi agrees that the reading in the large towns can be shifted to the day of assembly. Why doesn’t he also hold that large towns read the Megilla on the day of assembly when the fourteenth occurs on a Friday?
The Gemara responds: <b>How can</b> these cases <b>be compared? There,</b> in the second <i>baraita</i>, the designated <b>time for them</b> to read the Megilla <b>is Shabbat,</b> but the Megilla is not read on Shabbat, and therefore they must read it on a different day. Therefore, <b>since</b> the readings in the large towns have been <b>deferred, they are deferred</b> an additional day, and take place on Thursday, the day of assembly, at the same time as the readings in the villages. <b>Here, their</b> designated <b>time is Shabbat eve,</b> and there is no reason to move the reading from that day.
The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion <b>is that which Rabbi Ḥelbo said</b> that <b>Rav Huna said:</b> When <b>Purim occurs on Shabbat,</b> the reading of the Megilla in <b>all</b> places is <b>deferred to the day of assembly?</b> The Gemara corrects the wording of Rav Huna’s statement: <b>Can it enter your mind</b> to say that the reading of the Megilla in <b>all</b> places is deferred to the day of assembly? <b>Aren’t there walled cities that perform</b> this ceremony <b>the next day,</b> i.e., on Sunday? <b>Rather,</b> Rav Huna’s statement should say as follows: <b>All</b> readings <b>that are deferred are deferred to the day of assembly. In accordance with whose</b> opinion was this stated? It is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi.
<b>In any case,</b> it is apparent from the mishna and the <i>baraitot</i> that <b>everyone agrees that one does not read the</b> Megilla <b>on Shabbat. What is the reason</b> for this? <b>Rabba said: Everyone is obligated</b> to participate in <b>reading the Megilla</b> on Purim <b>and blowing the <i>shofar</i></b> on Rosh HaShana, <b>and not everyone is proficient in reading the Megilla.</b> Therefore, the Sages issued a rabbinic <b>decree</b> that the Megilla is not read on Shabbat, <b>lest one take</b> the Megilla <b>in his hand and go to an expert to learn</b> how to read it or to hear the expert read it, <b>and,</b> due to his preoccupation, he will <b>carry it four cubits in the public domain,</b> and thereby desecrate Shabbat.
The Gemara comments: <b>And this</b> same concern for the sanctity of Shabbat <b>is the reason</b> that the Sages decreed <b>that the <i>shofar</i></b> is not blown when Rosh HaShana occurs on Shabbat. <b>And this</b> same concern <b>is the reason</b> that the Sages decreed that one may not take <b>the <i>lulav</i></b> on Shabbat.
<b>Rav Yosef said</b> that there is another reason the Megilla is not read on Shabbat: <b>Because the eyes of the poor are raised to the reading of the Megilla.</b> The poor await the day on which the Megilla is read, because on that day gifts are distributed to the poor. If the Megilla is read on Shabbat, it will not be possible to distribute gifts to the poor, who will be deeply disappointed. The Gemara notes that <b>this is also taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Even though</b> the Sages <b>said that the villages advance</b> their reading of the Megilla <b>to the day of assembly, they</b> also <b>collect</b> the gifts for the poor <b>on that day, and they distribute</b> them to the poor <b>on that day.</b>
The Gemara is troubled by the wording of this <i>baraita</i>. Does the <i>baraita</i> read: <b>Even though</b> the Sages <b>said? On the contrary,</b> it is <b>because they said</b> that the villages advance their reading to the day of assembly that the gifts must be collected and distributed to the poor on that very day. <b>Rather,</b> the <i>baraita</i> should read as follows: <b>Since</b> the Sages <b>said that the villages advance</b> their reading of the Megilla <b>to the day of assembly, they collect</b> the gifts for the poor <b>on that day and they distribute</b> them <b>on that day, because the eyes of the poor are raised to the reading of the Megilla,</b> and they should not be disappointed. <b>However,</b>

Daf 5a

<b>the rejoicing</b> that takes place on Purim <b>is practiced only in its</b> designated <b>time,</b> the fourteenth of Adar.
§ <b>Rav said: One may read the Megilla in its</b> proper <b>time,</b> i.e., on the fourteenth of Adar, <b>even privately.</b> However, when it is read <b>not at its</b> proper <b>time,</b> e.g., when the villages advance their reading to the day of assembly, it must be read <b>with</b> a quorum of <b>ten,</b> because the enactment allowing the Megilla to be read before its proper time was only made for a community. <b>Rav Asi</b> disagreed and <b>said: Both at its</b> proper <b>time and not at its</b> proper <b>time,</b> the Megilla must be read <b>with</b> a quorum of <b>ten.</b> The Gemara relates that <b>there was an incident</b> where <b>Rav</b> had to read the Megilla on Purim, <b>and he was concerned for this</b> opinion of <b>Rav Asi</b> and gathered ten men even though he was reading the Megilla in its proper time, on the fourteenth of Adar.
The Gemara asks: <b>And did Rav</b> actually <b>say this,</b> that when the Megilla is read not at its proper time, it can only be read with a quorum of ten? <b>Didn’t Rav Yehuda, son of Rav Shmuel bar Sheilat, say in the name of Rav:</b> If <b>Purim occurs on Shabbat, Shabbat eve is</b> the proper <b>time</b> for reading the Megilla? The Gemara expresses surprise with regard to the wording of Rav’s statement: Is <b>Shabbat eve</b> the proper <b>time</b> for reading the Megilla? <b>Isn’t Shabbat</b> itself <b>its</b> proper <b>time? Rather, is it not</b> true <b>that this is what</b> he <b>said,</b> i.e., that this is the way his statement should be understood: Reading the Megilla <b>not at its</b> proper <b>time is like</b> reading it <b>at its</b> proper <b>time; just as at its</b> proper <b>time,</b> it can be read <b>even privately, so too, not at its</b> proper <b>time,</b> it can be read <b>even privately.</b>
The Gemara rejects this argument: Rav’s statement was <b>not</b> made <b>with regard to reading the Megilla with</b> a quorum of <b>ten. Rather, what</b> is the meaning of Rav’s statement that <b>Shabbat eve is</b> the proper <b>time?</b> It was meant <b>to exclude</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, <b>who said: Since</b> the readings in <b>the</b> large <b>towns were</b> already <b>deferred from their</b> usual <b>date</b> and the Megilla was not read on the fourteenth, <b>they are deferred to the day of assembly. This</b> statement of Rav <b>teaches us that Shabbat eve is</b> the proper <b>time</b> for these towns to read the Megilla, as stated in the mishna.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>What is</b> considered <b>a large city,</b> where the Megilla is read on the fourteenth of Adar? <b>Any</b> city <b>in which there are ten idlers.</b> However, if there are <b>fewer than that, it is</b> considered <b>a village,</b> even if it has many inhabitants.
<b>It was with regard to these</b> times for reading the Megilla that the Sages <b>said</b> that <b>one advances</b> the reading of the Megilla before the fourteenth of Adar <b>and one does not postpone</b> the reading to after its proper time. <b>However,</b> with regard to <b>the time</b> when families of <b>priests</b> donate <b>wood</b> for the fire on the altar, which were times those families would treat as Festivals; as well as the fast of the <b>Ninth of Av;</b> the <b>Festival</b> peace-offering that was brought on the Festivals; <b>and the</b> commandment of <b>assembly</b> [<b><i>hakhel</i></b>] of the entire Jewish people in the Temple courtyard on <i>Sukkot</i> in the year following the Sabbatical year to hear the king read the book of Deuteronomy; <b>one postpones</b> their observance until after Shabbat <b>and does not advance</b> their observance to before Shabbat.
The mishna continues: <b>Even though</b> the Sages <b>said</b> that <b>one advances</b> the time for reading the Megilla <b>and one does not postpone</b> the reading, one is <b>permitted</b> to <b>eulogize and fast</b> on these days, as they are not actually Purim; nevertheless, <b>gifts for the poor</b> are distributed on this day. <b>Rabbi Yehuda said: When</b> is the Megilla read on the day of assembly, before the fourteenth of Adar? In <b>a place where</b> the villagers generally <b>enter</b> town <b>on Monday and Thursday. However,</b> in <b>a place where they do not</b> generally <b>enter</b> town <b>on Monday and Thursday, one may read</b> the Megilla <b>only in its</b> designated <b>time,</b> the fourteenth of Adar.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> We learned in the mishna that a large city is one that has ten idlers. It was <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The <b>ten idlers</b> that are mentioned here are ten idlers <b>that are in the synagogue,</b> i.e., men who do not have professional responsibilities other than to sit in the synagogue and attend to communal religious needs. The presence of ten such men establishes a location as a prominent city.
We learned in the mishna: <b>It was with regard to these</b> times for reading the Megilla that the Sages <b>said</b> that <b>one advances</b> the reading of the Megilla <b>and one does not postpone</b> it. The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason</b> for this? <b>Rabbi Abba said</b> that <b>Shmuel said:</b> The <b>verse states:</b> “The Jews ordained, and took upon them, and upon their seed, and upon all who joined themselves to them, <b>and it shall not pass,</b> that they should keep these two days” (Esther 9:27), which indicates that the designated time must not pass without the reading of the Megilla.
Having mentioned a teaching of Rabbi Abba in the name of Shmuel, the Gemara cites another of his statements: <b>And Rabbi Abba said</b> that <b>Shmuel said: From where</b> is it derived <b>that one does not count days to</b> make up <b>years,</b> i.e., a year is considered to be comprised of either twelve or thirteen lunar months, and not 365 days? <b>As it is stated: “Of the months of the year”</b> (Exodus 12:2), which indicates that <b>you count months to</b> make up <b>years, but you do not count days</b> to make up <b>years.</b>
The Gemara adds: <b>And the Sages of Caesarea said in the name of Rabbi Abba: From where</b> is it derived <b>that one does not calculate hours to</b> reckon the <b>months?</b> A lunar cycle takes approximately twenty-nine and a half days, but a calendar month is considered to be twenty-nine or thirty full days and not precisely a lunar cycle. <b>As it is stated: “Until a month of days”</b> (Numbers 11:20), which indicates that <b>you calculate days to</b> reckon the <b>months, but you do not calculate hours to</b> reckon the <b>months.</b>
§ We learned in the mishna: <b>However,</b> with regard to <b>the time</b> when families of <b>priests</b> donate <b>wood</b> for the fire on the altar, the fast of <b>the Ninth of Av, the Festival</b> peace-offering, <b>and the</b> commandment of <b>assembly [<i>hakhel</i>], one postpones</b> their observance until after Shabbat <b>and does not advance</b> their observance to before Shabbat. The Gemara explains the reason for this <i>halakha</i> with respect to each item mentioned in the mishna. The fast of <b>the Ninth of Av</b> is not advanced because <b>one does not advance calamity;</b> since the Ninth of Av is a tragic time, its observance is postponed as long as possible. <b>The Festival</b> peace-offering <b>and the</b> commandment of <b>assembly [<i>hakhel</i>]</b> are not advanced <b>because the time of their obligation has not yet arrived,</b> and it is impossible to fulfill mitzvot before the designated time has arrived.
<b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One postpones the Festival</b> peace-offering <b>and the entire time</b> period <b>of the Festival</b> peace-offering. The Gemara attempts to clarify this statement: <b>Granted</b> that when the <i>baraita</i> says that the <b>Festival</b> peace-offering is postponed, it means <b>that if</b> a Festival <b>occurs on Shabbat,</b> when the Festival peace-offering cannot be sacrificed, <b>one postpones it until after Shabbat</b> and sacrifices the offering on the intermediate days of the Festival. <b>However, what is</b> the meaning of the phrase: <b>The time</b> period <b>of the Festival</b> peace-offering?
<b>Rav Oshaya said: This is what</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>is saying:</b> One postpones <b>the Festival</b> peace-offering if the Festival occurs <b>on Shabbat, and one postpones the</b> burnt-offering <b>of appearance even</b> due to <b>the Festival</b> itself. Despite the fact that a Festival day is <b>the time</b> for sacrificing a <b>Festival</b> peace-offering, the burnt-offering of appearance may not be sacrificed until after the Festival day.
The Gemara adds: <b>Whose</b> opinion is reflected in the mishna according to Rav Oshaya’s explanation? <b>It is</b> the opinion of <b>Beit Shammai, as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Beitza</i> 19a) that <b>Beit Shammai say: One may bring</b> peace-offerings <b>on a Festival</b> day to be sacrificed in the Temple. Most portions of a peace-offering are eaten by the priests and the individual who brought the offering. Consequently, its slaughter is considered food preparation, which is permitted on a Festival day. <b>And one may not place</b> his <b>hands</b> on the head of the offering, as that includes leaning with all one’s might upon the animal, which is prohibited on a Festival.
<b>However,</b> burnt-offerings <b>may not</b> be brought at all on the Festival. Since they are not eaten, their slaughter is not considered food preparation, and it therefore constitutes a prohibited labor on the Festival. <b>Beit Hillel</b> disagree and <b>say: One may bring</b> both peace-offerings <b>and</b> burnt-offerings on a Festival day, <b>and one may</b> even <b>place</b> his <b>hands on them.</b>
<b>Rava said</b> that the <i>baraita</i> should be understood as follows: <b>One postpones the Festival</b> peace-offering <b>for the entire time</b> period <b>of the Festival</b> peace-offering, i.e., for the entire duration of the Festival.
