However, it may <b>not</b> be postponed for <b>longer</b> than this. <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Ḥagiga</i> 9a): <b>One who did not offer the Festival</b> peace-offering <b>on the first Festival day of the festival</b> of <i>Sukkot</i> <b>may offer the Festival</b> peace-offering <b>for the</b> duration of the <b>entire pilgrimage Festival,</b> including the intermediate days <b>and the last day of the Festival.</b> If <b>the pilgrimage Festival has passed and he did not</b> yet <b>bring the Festival</b> peace-offering, <b>he is not obligated</b> to pay <b>restitution</b> for <b>it.</b> The obligation is no longer in force, and he therefore is not liable to bring another offering as compensation.
<b>Rav Ashi said</b> that the <i>baraita</i> should be understood as follows: <b>The Festival</b> peace-offering <b>may be postponed for the entire time</b> period <b>of a Festival</b> peace-offering. This indicates that <b>even</b> if <b><i>Shavuot</i>, which is one day,</b> occurs on Shabbat, <b>one postpones</b> the Festival peace-offering and offers it on one of the six days after <i>Shavuot</i>. <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Ḥagiga</i> 17a): Beit Hillel <b>concede that if <i>Shavuot</i> occurs on Shabbat, the day of slaughter is after Shabbat.</b> Since the Festival peace-offering and the burnt-offering of appearance cannot be sacrificed on Shabbat, they are slaughtered after Shabbat. This indicates that the Festival peace-offering may be slaughtered after the Festival day of <i>Shavuot</i>, as is the case on the other Festivals.
<b>Rabbi Elazar said</b> that <b>Rabbi Ḥanina said: Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi did several unusual things: <b>He planted a sapling on Purim,</b> and was not concerned about performing labor and thereby possibly denigrating the day.

Daf 5b

<b>And he bathed</b> at the time when the <b>wagons [<i>kerona</i>]</b> were traveling through <b>Tzippori,</b> i.e., on the market day, when the public would know about it, <b>on the seventeenth of Tammuz,</b> to show that bathing is permitted on that day. <b>And he sought to abolish</b> the fast of <b>the Ninth of Av. And</b> with respect to the Ninth of Av, the Sages <b>did not agree with him.</b>
<b>Rabbi Abba bar Zavda said to</b> Rabbi Elazar: <b>My teacher, the incident did not</b> occur in <b>this</b> fashion. Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi never sought to abolish the fast of the Ninth of Av. <b>Rather, it was</b> a year when <b>the Ninth of Av occurred on Shabbat, and they postponed it until after Shabbat. And Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>said</b> about that case: <b>Since it has</b> already <b>been deferred</b> from its usual time, <b>let it be</b> altogether <b>deferred</b> this year. <b>And the Rabbis did not agree with him.</b> Rabbi Elazar <b>read</b> the verse <b>about</b> Rabbi Abba bar Zavda: <b>“Two are better than one”</b> (Ecclesiastes 4:9), meaning, it is good that you were here to provide an accurate report about that incident.
The Gemara asks: <b>And how</b> could <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>plant a sapling on Purim? Didn’t Rav Yosef teach</b> with regard to the verse: “Therefore the Jews of the villages, who dwell in the unwalled towns, make the fourteenth day of the month of Adar a day of <b>gladness and feasting, and a good day [<i>yom tov</i>]”</b> (Esther 9:19), that the term <b>“gladness” teaches that</b> it is <b>prohibited</b> to <b>eulogize</b> on Purim; <b>“feasting” teaches that</b> it is <b>prohibited</b> to <b>fast; and</b> the term <b>“good day” [<i>yom tov</i>] teaches that</b> it is <b>prohibited</b> to <b>perform labor,</b> just as on a Festival, which is also referred to as a <i>yom tov</i>? <b>Rather,</b> what happened was as follows: <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi was in a place that observed Purim <b>on the fourteenth, and when he planted</b> the sapling, <b>he planted</b> it <b>on the fifteenth.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Is that so? Wasn’t Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>in Tiberias, and Tiberias was surrounded by a wall since the days of Joshua, son of Nun.</b> Consequently, he was obligated to observe Purim on the fifteenth. <b>Rather,</b> say just the opposite: <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi lived in a place that observed Purim <b>on the fifteenth, and when he planted</b> the sapling, <b>he planted</b> it <b>on the fourteenth.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Was it obvious to</b> Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi <b>that</b> the city of <b>Tiberias was surrounded by a wall since the days of Joshua, son of Nun? Didn’t Hezekiah read</b> the Megilla <b>in Tiberias</b> both <b>on the fourteenth and on the fifteenth</b> of Adar, because <b>he was uncertain if</b> it had been <b>surrounded by a wall since the days of Joshua, son of Nun, or not?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Hezekiah was</b> indeed <b>uncertain</b> about the matter, whereas <b>it was obvious to Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi that Tiberias had been surrounded by a wall in the time of Joshua.
The Gemara asks further: <b>And when it was obvious to</b> Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi that the Megilla should be read in Tiberias on the fifteenth, <b>was it permitted</b> to plant there on the fourteenth? <b>Isn’t it written in <i>Megillat Ta’anit</i></b> that <b>the fourteenth day and the fifteenth day of Adar are the days of Purim,</b> and <b>one is not</b> permitted <b>to eulogize on them?</b>
<b>And Rava said:</b> This statement <b>is necessary only to prohibit</b> those who observe Purim <b>on this</b> day to eulogize <b>on that</b> day, <b>and</b> those who observe Purim <b>on that</b> day to eulogize <b>on this</b> day. Since the two days are mentioned in the Bible, it was only necessary to mention them in <i>Megillat Ta’anit</i> in order to indicate that the prohibition against eulogizing applies to both days. Presumably, the same should apply to the prohibition against performing labor. Consequently, how could Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi plant a sapling on the fourteenth of Adar? The Gemara answers: <b>That applies</b> only to <b>eulogies and fasting. However, labor</b> is prohibited for only <b>one day,</b> either the fourteenth or the fifteenth, <b>and no more.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Is that so? Didn’t Rav see a certain man planting flax on Purim, and cursed him, and</b> the man’s <b>flax never grew.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>There,</b> the man <b>was</b> obligated to observe Purim <b>on that day</b> that he planted the flax. Therefore, it was certainly prohibited to perform labor.
<b>Rabba, son of Rava, said</b> a different answer to the question: <b>Even</b> if <b>you say</b> that Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi planted the sapling <b>on his</b> own <b>day</b> of Purim, i.e., on the day that the Megilla was read in his location, it was still permitted to plant the sapling. This is because the Jewish people <b>accepted upon themselves</b> the prohibitions against <b>eulogizing and fasting</b> on Purim, but <b>they did not accept upon themselves the prohibition</b> against performing <b>labor.</b>
This can be proven from the fact <b>that initially,</b> when Mordecai and Esther proposed the celebration of Purim, <b>it is written:</b> “A day of <b>gladness and feasting and a good day [<i>yom tov</i>]”</b> (Esther 9:19), <b>and at the end,</b> when the celebration of Purim was accepted by the Jewish people, <b>it is written:</b> “That they should <b>make them days of feasting and gladness”</b> (Esther 9:22), <b>whereas</b> the term <b>good day [<i>yom tov</i>],</b> which alludes to a day when it is prohibited to perform labor, <b>is not written.</b> The people never accepted upon themselves the prohibition against performing labor on Purim as if it were a Festival, and therefore the prohibition never took effect.
The Gemara asks: If labor is permitted on Purim, <b>what is the reason that Rav cursed that man</b> who planted the flax? The Gemara answers: <b>It was</b> a case of <b>matters that are permitted</b> by <i>halakha</i>, <b>but others were accustomed to</b> treat <b>them as a prohibition,</b> in which case one may not permit these actions in their presence, lest they come to treat other prohibitions lightly. In the place where that man planted his flax, it was customary to abstain from labor on Purim. However, <b>in Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi’s <b>place, it was not the custom</b> to abstain from labor on Purim, and therefore it was permitted for him to plant the sapling even in public.
<b>And if you wish, say</b> an alternative answer: <b>Actually, it was the custom</b> to abstain from labor on Purim in Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi’s place, <b>and Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi engaged in a <b>joyful</b> act of <b>planting,</b> for pleasure rather than for financial benefit. <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna with regard to public fasts: If <b>these</b> fasts for rain <b>have passed and</b> the community’s prayers <b>have</b> still <b>not been answered,</b> and the drought continues, <b>one decreases his business</b> activities, as well as <b>construction, planting, betrothals, and marriages.</b>
<b>And it was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>about this</b> mishna: When the Sages said that <b>construction</b> must be decreased on public fasts, they were not referring to the construction of homes for people who have nowhere to live, but to <b>joyful construction.</b> Similarly, when they said that <b>planting</b> must be decreased, they were not referring to planting food crops, but to <b>joyful planting. What is</b> meant by <b>joyful construction? This</b> is referring to <b>one who builds a wedding chamber for his son.</b> It was customary to build a special house where the wedding would take place, and at times the couple would also live there. <b>What is</b> meant by <b>joyful planting? This</b> is referring to <b>one who plants</b> trees for shade and pleasure such as one might find in <b>a royal garden</b> [<b><i>avurneki</i></b>]. Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi engaged in joyful planting on Purim, in keeping with the joyous nature of the day.
§ The Gemara examines <b>the</b> matter <b>itself</b> cited in the previous discussion. <b>Hezekiah read</b> the Megilla <b>in Tiberias</b> both <b>on the fourteenth and on the fifteenth</b> of Adar, because <b>he was uncertain if it</b> had been <b>surrounded by a wall since the days of Joshua, son of Nun, or not.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Was he</b> really <b>uncertain about the matter of Tiberias? Isn’t it written: “And the fortified cities were Ziddim-zer, and Hammath, Rakkath, and Chinnereth”</b> (Joshua 19:35)<b>, and we maintain</b> that <b>Rakkath is Tiberias?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>This is the reason that he was uncertain:</b> Although Tiberias was surrounded by a wall in the time of Joshua, Hezekiah was uncertain about the <i>halakha</i> <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that</b> on <b>one side, there was a wall of the sea,</b> i.e., there was no physical wall, but the city was protected due to the fact that it adjoined the sea.
The Gemara asks: <b>If so, why was he uncertain?</b> The sea is <b>certainly not a wall. As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to the sale of houses in walled cities, the phrase: <b>“Which has a wall”</b> (Leviticus 25:30), indicates that the city has a bona fide wall <b>and not</b> merely <b>a wall of roofs.</b> If a city is completely encircled by attached houses but there is no separate wall, it is not considered a walled city. The next verse, which is referring to cities that have no wall <b>“round about</b> them” (Leviticus 25:31), <b>excludes Tiberias</b> from being considered a walled city, <b>as the sea is its wall</b> on one side and it is not fully encircled by a physical wall. Consequently, Tiberias is not considered a walled city.
The Gemara answers: <b>With regard to</b> the sale of <b>houses of walled cities,</b> Hezekiah <b>was not uncertain. Where he was uncertain was with regard to the reading of the Megilla: What are the unwalled towns and what are the walled cities that are written with regard to the reading of the Megilla?</b> Is the difference between them <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that these</b> unwalled towns <b>are exposed, whereas those</b> walled cities <b>are not exposed?</b> If so, since Tiberias <b>is also exposed,</b> as it is not entirely surrounded by a wall, it should be considered unwalled. <b>Or perhaps</b> the difference is <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that these</b> walled cities <b>are protected, whereas those</b> unwalled towns <b>are not protected, and</b> Tiberias <b>is also protected</b> by the sea and should be treated as a walled city. It was <b>due to that</b> reason that Hezekiah <b>was uncertain</b> when to read the Megilla.
The Gemara relates that <b>Rav Asi read the Megilla in</b> the city of <b>Huzal</b> in Babylonia on both <b>the fourteenth and the fifteenth</b> of Adar, because <b>he was uncertain if</b> it had been <b>surrounded</b> by a wall <b>since the days of Joshua, son of Nun, or not.</b> Huzal was an ancient city, and it was possible that it had been surrounded by a wall in the time of Joshua. <b>Some say</b> a different version of this report, according to which there was no uncertainty. <b>Rav Asi said: This</b> city of <b>Huzal of the house of Benjamin was walled since the days of Joshua, son of Nun.</b>
Incidental to the previous discussion concerning Tiberias, the Gemara relates that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: When I was a child I said something that I</b> later <b>asked the Elders</b> about,

Daf 6a

<b>and it was found in accordance with my</b> opinion. I said that <b>Hammath is Tiberias. And why was it called Hammath? On account of the hot springs of [<i>ḥammei</i>] Tiberias</b> that are located there. And I said that <b>Rakkath is Tzippori. And why was it called Rakkath? Because it is raised</b> above the surrounding areas <b>like the bank [<i>rakta</i>] of a river.</b> And I said that <b>Chinnereth is Ginosar. And why was it called Chinnereth? Because its fruit are sweet like the sound of a harp [<i>kinnor</i>].</b>
<b>Rava said: Is there anyone who says that Rakkath is not Tiberias? Isn’t</b> it true that <b>when a</b> great <b>man dies here,</b> in Babylonia, <b>they lament his</b> demise <b>there,</b> in Tiberias, <b>as follows: Great was he in Sheshakh,</b> i.e., Babylonia (see Jeremiah 25:26), <b>and he had a name in Rakkath?</b> Furthermore, <b>when they bring up the casket</b> of an important person <b>to there,</b> to Tiberias, <b>they lament his</b> demise <b>as follows:</b> You <b>lovers</b> of <b>the remnants</b> of the Jewish people, <b>residents of Rakkath, go out and receive the dead from the deep,</b> i.e., the low-lying lands of Babylonia.
Similarly, the Gemara relates that <b>when Rabbi Zeira died, a certain eulogizer opened</b> his eulogy <b>for him</b> with these words: <b>The land of Shinar,</b> i.e., Babylonia, Rabbi Zeira’s birthplace, <b>conceived and bore</b> him; <b>the land of the deer,</b> i.e., Eretz Yisrael, where Rabbi Zeira lived as an adult and rose to prominence, <b>raised her delights. Woe unto her, said Rakkath, for she has lost her precious instrument.</b> It is apparent from these examples that Rakkath is Tiberias.
<b>Rather, Rabba said: Hammath is the hot springs of Gerar</b> that are adjacent to Tiberias; <b>Rakkath is Tiberias;</b> and <b>Chinnereth is Ginosar. And why was</b> Tiberias <b>called Rakkath? Because even the empty ones [<i>reikanin</i>] of</b> Tiberias <b>are</b> as <b>full of mitzvot as a pomegranate</b> is full of seeds. <b>Rabbi Yirmeya said:</b> In fact, <b>Rakkath is its</b> real <b>name; and why was it called Tiberias? Because it sits in the</b> very <b>center</b> [<b><i>tabbur</i></b>] <b>of Eretz Yisrael. Rava said: Rakkath is its</b> real <b>name, and why was it called Tiberias? Because its appearance is good [<i>tova re’iyyata</i></b>].
§ While continuing to identify places that are mentioned in the Bible, <b>Zeira said:</b> The city of <b>Kitron</b> that is mentioned in the Bible <b>is</b> the city of <b>Tzippori. And why was it called Tzippori? Because it sits on top of a mountain like a bird [<i>tzippor</i>].</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Is Kitron</b> really <b>Tzippori? Wasn’t Kitron in the</b> tribal <b>territory of Zebulun, as it is written: “Neither did Zebulun drive out the inhabitants of Kitron, nor the inhabitants of Nahalol”</b> (Judges 1:30)? <b>And</b> the tribe of <b>Zebulun was resentful of its portion, as it is stated: “Zebulun was a people that jeopardized their lives to the death”</b> (Judges 5:18). <b>What is the reason</b> for their resentfulness? <b>Because “Naphtali was on the high places of the field”</b> (Judges 5:18).
The verse should be interpreted as follows: <b>Zebulun said before the Holy One, Blessed be He: Master of the Universe! To my brothers,</b> the tribes whose territory is adjacent to mine, <b>You gave fields and vineyards, whereas to me You gave mountains and hills; to my brothers You gave lands, whereas to me You gave seas and rivers.</b> God <b>said</b> back <b>to him:</b> Nevertheless, <b>all will need you due to the <i>ḥilazon</i>,</b> the small sea creature residing in your territory that is the source of the dye used in the ritual fringes [<i>tzitzit</i>]. <b>As it is stated</b> in Moses’ blessing to Zebulun: <b>“They shall call the people to the mountain:</b> There they shall sacrifice offerings of righteousness; for they shall suck of the abundance of the seas, <b>and of the hidden treasures of the sand”</b> (Deuteronomy 33:19).
<b>Rav Yosef teaches</b> about this: <b>“Treasures”; this is</b> referring to <b>the <i>ḥilazon</i>,</b> which is found in the waters of Zebulun. <b>“Hidden”; this is</b> referring to the <b><i>tarit</i>,</b> a type of sardine, which is also found in Zebulun’s coastal waters. <b>“Sand”; this is</b> referring to the sand from which <b>white glass</b> is made. Zebulun <b>said to Him:</b> All of these resources are indeed found in my territory, but <b>Master of the Universe, who will inform me</b> if others take them without permission? <b>He said to</b> the tribe of Zebulun: <b>“There they shall sacrifice offerings of righteousness.” This shall be a sign for you</b> that <b>anyone who takes</b> these items <b>from you without</b> making <b>payment will not prosper at all in his business.</b>
It is clear from the exposition of the verse in Judges that the territory of Zebulun did not contain fields and vineyards. <b>And if it enters your mind</b> to say that <b>Kitron is Tzippori, why was</b> Zebulun <b>resentful of his portion? Wasn’t Tzippori</b> in his territory, which was land that was <b>vastly superior</b> with regard to its produce? <b>And if you would say that</b> Zebulun’s portion <b>did not have</b> quality land <b>flowing with milk and honey, didn’t Reish Lakish say: I myself have seen</b> the land <b>flowing with milk and honey</b> around <b>Tzippori, and it was sixteen <i>mil</i> by sixteen <i>mil</i>?</b>
<b>And if you would say that</b> the part of <b>his</b> territory that flowed with milk and honey <b>was not as vast as</b> that of <b>his brothers,</b> the other tribes, <b>didn’t Rabba bar bar Ḥana say</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: I myself have seen</b> the land <b>flowing with milk and honey</b> over <b>all of Eretz Yisrael. And</b> the size of the fertile land <b>was like</b> the distance <b>from Bei Kovei to the fortress of Tulbakni,</b> a total of <b>twenty-two parasangs [<i>parsa</i>] in length and six parasangs in width.</b> A parasang is four <i>mil</i>; consequently, the area flowing with milk and honey around Tzippori was four by four parasangs, which is more than the fair share of one tribe among twelve.
The Gemara answers: <b>Even so, fields and vineyards were preferable to</b> Zebulun. The fertile land in Zebulun’s territory is in a mountainous region, which makes it more difficult to cultivate. The Gemara comments: The language of the verse <b>is also precise</b> according to this explanation, <b>as it is written: “And Naphtali was on the high places of the field,”</b> which indicates that Zebulun’s complaint was due to the fact that Naphtali had fields. The Gemara concludes: Indeed, <b>learn from here</b> that this is so.
§ The Gemara continues its discussion with regard to identifying places mentioned in the Bible. <b>Rabbi Abbahu said: “And Ekron shall be uprooted”</b> (Zephaniah 2:4). <b>This is</b> an allusion to <b>Caesarea, daughter of Edom, which is situated among the sands.</b> Caesarea was primarily populated by Greeks and Romans, and it served as the seat of Roman rule when the Romans, who are identified with Edom in Jewish literature, ruled Eretz Yisrael. <b>And it was a spike stuck in</b> the side of <b>the Jewish people</b> already <b>in the days of the Greeks,</b> as it was an obstacle to the spread of Jewish settlement. <b>When the Hasmonean monarchy prevailed and triumphed over them, they called it: The captured tower of Shir.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yosei bar Ḥanina said: What is</b> the meaning of that <b>which is written: “And I will take away his blood out of his mouth, and his detestable things from between his teeth, and he also shall be a remnant for our God;</b> and he shall be as a chief in Judah, and Ekron as a Jebusite” (Zechariah 9:7)? The verse should be understood as follows: <b>“And I will take away his blood out of his mouth”; this is</b> referring to <b>their house of altars,</b> where they sacrifice offerings. <b>“And his detestable things from between his teeth”; this is</b> referring to <b>their house of piles,</b> where they heap their ritual stones.
<b>“And he also shall be a remnant for our God,” these</b> words <b>are</b> referring to <b>the synagogues and study halls in Edom. “And he shall be as a chief</b> [<b><i>aluf</i></b> ] <b>in Judah, and Ekron as a Jebusite,” these</b> words <b>are</b> referring to <b>the theaters [<i>tere’atrayot</i>] and the circuses [<i>kirkesayot</i>] in Edom where the officers of Judah are destined to teach Torah in public.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yitzḥak said:</b> “And the children of Dan went up and fought against <b>Leshem”</b> (Joshua 19:47); <b>this is</b> referring to the city that was known in the Talmudic period as <b>Pamyas. “Ekron shall be uprooted”</b> (Zephaniah 2:4); <b>this is</b> referring to <b>Caesarea, the daughter of Edom, which was a metropolis [<i>metropolin</i>],</b> i.e., a capital city, <b>of kings. There are</b> those <b>who say</b> this means <b>that kings were raised there, and there are</b> those <b>who say</b> it means <b>that kings were appointed from there,</b> meaning the kings of Edom were appointed from among the residents of this city.
The Sages said that the fortunes of <b>Caesarea,</b> which represents Rome, <b>and Jerusalem</b> are diametric opposites. <b>If,</b> therefore, <b>someone says to you</b> that <b>both</b> cities <b>are destroyed, do not believe</b> him. Similarly, if he says to you that <b>they are both settled</b> in tranquility, <b>do not believe</b> him. If, however, he says to you that <b>Caesarea is destroyed and Jerusalem is settled,</b> or that <b>Jerusalem is destroyed and Caesarea is settled, believe</b> him. <b>As it is stated:</b> “Because Tyre has said against Jerusalem: Aha, the gates of the people have been broken; she is turned to me; <b>I shall be filled with her that is laid waste”</b> (Ezekiel 26:2), and Tyre, like Caesarea, represents Rome. Consequently, the verse indicates that <b>if this</b> city <b>is filled, that one is laid waste,</b> and <b>if</b> that city <b>is filled, this one is laid waste.</b> The two cities cannot coexist.
<b>Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak said:</b> The same idea may be derived <b>from here,</b> a verse dealing with Jacob and Esau: <b>“And the one people shall be stronger than the other people”</b> (Genesis 25:23), teaching that when one nation rises, the other necessarily falls.
§ Having mentioned Edom, the Gemara cites what <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak said: What is</b> the meaning of that <b>which is written: “Let favor be shown to the wicked, yet will he not learn righteousness;</b> in the land of uprightness he will deal wrongfully, and will not behold the majesty of the Lord” (Isaiah 26:10)? <b>Isaac said before the Holy One, Blessed be He: Master of the Universe, let favor be shown to Esau,</b> my beloved son. God <b>said to him:</b> Esau <b>is wicked.</b> Isaac <b>said to</b> God: <b>“Yet will he not learn righteousness,”</b> i.e., is there no one who can find merit in him? God <b>said to him: “In the land of uprightness he will deal wrongfully,”</b> meaning that he is destined to destroy Eretz Yisrael. Isaac <b>said to</b> God: <b>If</b> it is <b>so</b> that he is that wicked, <b>“he will not behold the majesty of the Lord.”</b>
<b>And Rabbi Yitzḥak</b> also <b>said: What is</b> the meaning of that <b>which is written: “Grant not, O Lord, the desires of the wicked; further not his evil device, so that they not exalt themselves. Selah”</b> (Psalms 140:9)? <b>Jacob said before the Holy One, Blessed be He: Master of the Universe, grant not to the wicked Esau the desires of his heart,</b> as he wishes to destroy us. <b>Further not his evil device [<i>zemamo</i>];</b> do not remove the muzzle [<i>zamam</i>] that constrains him and prevents him from breaking out and gathering further strength. <b>This is</b> a reference to

Daf 6b

<b>Germamya of Edom,</b> i.e., Germany, which is near the land of Edom, i.e., Rome. <b>As, if</b> the Germans <b>would go forth, they would destroy the entire world.</b>
<b>And Rabbi Ḥama Bar Ḥanina said: There are three hundred</b> young princes with <b>crowns tied</b> to their heads <b>in Germamya of Edom, and there are three hundred and sixty-five chieftains [<i>marzavnei</i>] in Rome. Every day these go out</b> to battle <b>against those, and one of them is killed, and they are preoccupied with appointing a</b> new <b>king</b> in his place. Since neither side is united, neither side is able to achieve a decisive victory. It is these wars between Rome and the Germanic tribes that act as a muzzle upon Esau-Edom-Rome and prevent it from becoming too strong.
§ <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak said</b> in the style of a previous passage: <b>If a person says to you: I have labored and not found</b> success, <b>do not believe</b> him. Similarly, if he says to you: <b>I have not labored but</b> nevertheless <b>I have found</b> success, <b>do not believe</b> him. If, however, he says to you: <b>I have labored and I have found</b> success, <b>believe</b> him.
The Gemara comments: <b>This applies</b> only <b>to matters of Torah,</b> as success with respect to Torah study is in accordance with the toil and effort invested. <b>But with regard to</b> success in <b>business, it</b> all depends upon <b>assistance from Heaven,</b> as there is no correlation between success and effort. <b>And</b> even <b>with regard to matters of Torah, we said</b> this <b>only with regard to sharpening</b> one’s understanding of Torah, as the more one labors, the deeper the understanding of the material he achieves. <b>However, to preserve what one has learned,</b> it <b>is</b> dependent upon <b>assistance from Heaven.</b> Not everyone achieves this, even with much effort.
<b>And Rabbi Yitzḥak</b> also <b>said: If you see a wicked man whom the hour is smiling upon,</b> i.e., who is enjoying good fortune, <b>do not provoke him, as it is stated: “Contend not with evildoers”</b> (Psalms 37:1). <b>And not only that, but</b> if you provoke him, <b>his undertakings will be successful, as it is stated: “His ways prosper at all times”</b> (Psalms 10:5). <b>And not only that, but</b> even if he is brought to court, <b>he emerges victorious in judgment, as it is stated: “Your judgments are far above him”</b> (Psalms 10:5), as though the trial is far removed from him and does not affect him. <b>And not only that, but he will see his enemies</b> fall, <b>as it is stated: “As for all his enemies, he hisses at them”</b> (Psalms 10:5).
The Gemara asks: <b>Is that so? Didn’t Rabbi Yoḥanan say in the name of Rabbi Shimon ben Yoḥai: It is permitted to provoke the wicked in this world, as it is stated: “They that forsake the Torah praise the wicked; but they who keep the Torah contend with them”</b> (Proverbs 28:4)? <b>And</b> furthermore, <b>it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Dostai bar Matun said: It is permitted to provoke the wicked in this world, and if a person whispers to you to say</b> that this is not so, relying on the verse: <b>“Contend not with evildoers, nor be envious against the workers of iniquity” (</b>Psalms 37:1), know that only <b>one whose heart strikes him</b> with pangs of conscience over sins that he committed <b>says this.</b>
<b>Rather,</b> the true meaning of that verse is: <b>“Contend not with evildoers,” to be like the evildoers; “nor be envious against the workers of iniquity,” to be like the workers of iniquity. And it says</b> elsewhere: <b>“Let not your heart envy sinners,</b> but be in the fear of the Lord all the day” (Proverbs 23:17). In this context, to be envious of sinners means to desire to be like them. Rabbi Yoḥanan and Rabbi Dostai indicate that one is permitted to provoke the wicked, against the opinion of Rabbi Yitzḥak.
The Gemara explains: This is <b>not difficult,</b> as it can be understood that <b>this,</b> Rabbi Yitzḥak’s statement that one may not provoke the wicked, is referring <b>to his</b> personal <b>matters,</b> while <b>that,</b> the statements of Rabbi Yoḥanan and Rabbi Dostai that it is permitted to provoke them, is referring <b>to matters of Heaven,</b> i.e., religious matters.
<b>And if you wish, say:</b> Both <b>this</b> statement <b>and that</b> statement are stated <b>with regard to his own affairs, and</b> still it is <b>not difficult. This</b> statement, that it is permitted to provoke the wicked, <b>applies to a completely righteous</b> individual; <b>that</b> statement, that one may not provoke them, <b>applies to</b> an individual <b>who is not completely righteous. As Rav Huna said: What is</b> the meaning of that <b>which is written: “Why do you look upon them that deal treacherously, and remain silent when the wicked devours the man that is more righteous than he”</b> (Habakkuk 1:13)? This verse indicates that the wicked <b>devours one who is more righteous than he;</b> however, <b>he does not devour one who is completely righteous.</b>
<b>And if you wish, say</b> instead: When the <b>hour is smiling upon him,</b> i.e., when the wicked individual is enjoying good fortune, it <b>is different.</b> He is receiving divine assistance, and even the completely righteous should not provoke him.
§ Having mentioned Rome, the Gemara cites what <b>Ulla said. Greek Italy,</b> i.e., southern Italy, <b>is the great city of Rome, and it is three hundred parasang [<i>parsa</i>] by three hundred parasang. It has three hundred and sixty-five markets, corresponding to the number of days in the solar</b> year, <b>and the smallest of them all is</b> the market <b>of poultry sellers,</b> which <b>is sixteen <i>mil</i> by sixteen <i>mil</i>. And the king,</b> i.e., the Roman emperor, <b>dines every day in one of them.</b>
<b>And one who resides in</b> the city, <b>even if he was not born there, receives an allowance</b> for his living expenses <b>from the king’s palace. And one who was born there, even if he does not reside there,</b> also <b>receives an allowance from the king’s palace. And there are three thousand bathhouses in</b> the city, <b>and five hundred apertures that let the smoke</b> from the bathhouses <b>out beyond the walls</b> in a way that doesn’t blacken the walls themselves. <b>One side</b> of the city is bordered by the <b>sea, one side</b> by <b>mountains and hills, one side</b> by <b>a barrier of iron and one side</b> by <b>gravel [<i>ḥulsit</i>] and swamp.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If the people <b>read the Megilla during the first Adar and</b> subsequently <b>the year was</b> then <b>intercalated</b> by the court and now the following month will be the second Adar, <b>one reads</b> the Megilla again <b>during the second Adar.</b> The Sages formulated a principle: <b>The difference between the first Adar and the second Adar</b> with regard to the mitzvot that are performed during those months <b>is only</b> that <b>the reading of the Megilla and</b> distributing <b>gifts to the poor</b> are performed in the second Adar and not in the first Adar.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara infers <b>that with regard to the matter of the sequence of</b> Torah <b>portions</b> read each year on two <i>Shabbatot</i> before Purim, the portions of <i>Shekalim</i> and <i>Zakhor</i>, and on two <i>Shabbatot</i> after Purim, <i>Para</i> and <i>HaḤodesh</i>, <b>this,</b> the first Adar, <b>and that,</b> the second Adar <b>are equal,</b> in that reading them during the first Adar exempts one from reading them in the second Adar.
The Gemara asks: If so, <b>whose</b> opinion is taught in <b>the mishna? It is neither</b> the opinion of <b>the</b> anonymous <b>first <i>tanna</i></b> of the following <i>baraita</i>, <b>nor</b> that of <b>Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Yosei, nor</b> that of <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If <b>they read the Megilla during the first Adar and the year was</b> then <b>intercalated, they read it during the second Adar, as all mitzvot that are practiced during the second</b> Adar <b>are practiced in the first</b> Adar, <b>except for the reading of the Megilla.</b>
<b>Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Yosei, says: They do not read it</b> again <b>during the second Adar, as all mitzvot that are practiced during the second</b> Adar <b>are practiced during the first</b> Adar. Once the Megilla was read during the first Adar, one need not read it again during the second Adar.
<b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel says in the name of Rabbi Yosei: They even read it</b> again <b>during the second Adar, as all mitzvot that are practiced during the second</b> Adar <b>are not practiced during the first</b> Adar. <b>And</b> they all <b>agree with regard to eulogy and with regard to fasting that they are prohibited on</b> the fourteenth and the fifteenth days of <b>this</b> month of the first Adar <b>and on that</b> month of the second Adar.
The Gemara analyzes the <i>baraita</i>. The opinion of <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel is</b> identical to <b>that</b> of <b>the first <i>tanna</i>.</b> What novel element does he introduce? <b>Rav Pappa said: There is</b> a practical difference <b>between them</b> with regard to <b>the sequence of</b> four Torah <b>portions, as the first <i>tanna</i> maintains:</b> They should read those portions <b>during the second</b> Adar, <b><i>ab initio</i>. However, if they did</b> so <b>during the first</b> Adar, <b>they did</b> so; and they fulfilled their obligation and need not read them again during the second Adar, <b>except for the reading of the Megilla, as even though they</b> already <b>read</b> it <b>during the first</b> Adar, <b>they read it</b> again <b>during the second</b> Adar.
<b>And Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Yosei, maintains</b> that <b>even the reading of the Megilla</b> may be performed <b>during the first</b> Adar, <b><i>ab initio</i>,</b> and they need not read it again during the second Adar. <b>And Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel maintains: Even</b> with regard to <b>the sequence of</b> four Torah <b>portions, if they read</b> them <b>during the first</b> Adar, <b>they read</b> them again <b>during the second</b> Adar.
Returning to the original question, according to <b>whose</b> opinion is the mishna taught? <b>If</b> it is the opinion of <b>the first <i>tanna</i>,</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>gifts</b> to the poor is <b>difficult.</b> The first <i>tanna</i> does not mention these gifts, indicating that he maintains that if gifts were distributed during the first Adar one need not distribute gifts to the poor during the second Adar. And <b>if</b> the mishna was taught according to the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Yosei, the reading of the Megilla</b> is <b>also difficult.</b> And <b>if</b> it is the opinion of <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel, the sequence of</b> Torah <b>portions</b> is <b>difficult.</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>Actually,</b> the mishna is according to the opinion of <b>the first <i>tanna</i>, and he taught</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>the reading of the Megilla, and the same is true</b> with regard to <b>gifts to the poor, as this</b> mitzva <b>is dependent upon that</b> one. The Gemara already explained that the gifts to the poor are distributed on the day that the Megilla is read.
<b>And if you wish, say</b> instead: <b>Actually,</b> the mishna <b>is</b> according to the opinion of <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel, and the mishna is incomplete and is teaching the following: The difference between the fourteenth</b> day <b>of the first Adar and the fourteenth</b> day <b>of the second Adar is only</b> with regard to <b>the reading of the Megilla and</b> distributing <b>gifts</b> to the poor. The Gemara infers <b>that</b> with regard to <b>the matter of eulogy and fasting, this,</b> the first Adar, <b>and that,</b> the second Adar <b>are equal, while</b> about <b>the sequence of</b> Torah <b>portions,</b> the mishna <b>does not speak</b> at all. The mishna limits its discussion to the <i>halakhot</i> of Purim.
<b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Avin said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel, who said</b> it <b>in the name of Rabbi Yosei.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: And both of them,</b> Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel and Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Yosei, <b>interpreted the same verse</b> differently, leading them to their conclusions. It is written: “To enjoin upon them that they should keep the fourteenth day of the month of Adar and the fifteenth day of the same, <b>in each and every year”</b> (Esther 9:21). <b>Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Yosei, maintains: “In each and every year”</b> teaches that Purim must be celebrated the same way each year, even if it is intercalated: <b>Just as each and every year</b> Purim is celebrated during <b>Adar that is adjacent to Shevat, so too here</b> in an intercalated year Purim is celebrated during <b>Adar that is adjacent to Shevat.</b>
<b>And Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel maintains: “In each and every year”</b> teaches that <b>just as each and every year</b> Purim is celebrated in <b>Adar that is adjacent to Nisan, so too here,</b> in an intercalated year, Purim is celebrated during <b>Adar that is adjacent to Nisan.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Granted,</b> according to <b>Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Yosei, the reason</b> for his opinion <b>is logical,</b> based on the principle that <b>one does not forego</b> performance of <b>the mitzvot;</b> rather, when presented with the opportunity to perform a mitzva, one should do so immediately. <b>However,</b> with regard to <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel, what is the reason</b> for his opinion?
<b>Rabbi Tavi said: The reason</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel</b> is that <b>juxtaposing</b> the celebration of one <b>redemption,</b> Purim, <b>to</b> the celebration of another <b>redemption,</b> Passover, <b>is preferable.</b>
<b>Rabbi Elazar said: The reason</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel</b> is derived <b>from here, as it is written: “To confirm this second letter of Purim”</b> (Esther 9:29), indicating that there are circumstances where the Megilla is read a second time (Jerusalem Talmud), i.e., when the year was intercalated after the Megilla was read in the first Adar.
The Gemara comments: <b>And it was necessary to write</b>

Daf 7a

the term: <b>The second, and it was</b> also <b>necessary to write</b> the phrase: <b>In each and every year;</b> proof from one of the verses would have been insufficient. <b>As, if</b> I had derived the <i>halakha</i> only <b>from</b> the phrase: <b>In each and every year, I would have said</b> my conclusion <b>according to our question</b> raised earlier: Why not celebrate Purim in the Adar adjacent to Shevat? <b>Therefore, it teaches us</b> using the term: <b>The second. And had it taught us</b> only the term: <b>The second, I would have said</b> that Purim must be celebrated both <b>in the first</b> Adar <b>and in the second</b> Adar, <b><i>ab initio</i>. Therefore, it teaches us: In each and every year,</b> indicating that even in an intercalated year, just as in an ordinary year, Purim is to be celebrated only once.
The Gemara asks: <b>And Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Yosei, what does he do with this</b> term: <b>The second?</b> Since he holds that the Megilla is read in the first Adar, what does he derive from the verse? The Gemara answers: <b>He requires</b> the term <b>to</b> derive <b>that</b> statement <b>of Rav Shmuel bar Yehuda, as Rav Shmuel bar Yehuda said: Initially, they established</b> the observance of Purim <b>in</b> the city of <b>Shushan</b> alone, <b>and ultimately</b> they established it <b>throughout the world,</b> according to the second letter of Purim.
Apropos the statement of Rav Shmuel bar Yehuda with regard to the establishment of the holiday of Purim, the Gemara cites a related statement. <b>Rav Shmuel bar Yehuda said: Esther sent to the Sages: Establish me for</b> future <b>generations.</b> Esther requested that the observance of Purim and the reading of the Megilla be instituted as an ordinance for all generations. <b>They sent to her: You will</b> thereby <b>arouse the wrath of the nations upon us,</b> as the Megilla recounts the victory of the Jews over the gentiles, and it is best not to publicize that victory. <b>She sent</b> back <b>to them: I am already written in the chronicles of the kings of Media and Persia,</b> and so the Megilla will not publicize anything that is not already known worldwide.
It was related that <b>Rav and Rabbi Ḥanina and Rabbi Yoḥanan and Rav Ḥaviva taught</b> the statement cited below. The Gemara comments: <b>Throughout the order of <i>Moed</i>, wherever this</b> latter <b>pair</b> of Sages is mentioned, <b>exchange Rabbi Yoḥanan and insert Rabbi Yonatan</b> in his place. They said: <b>Esther sent to the Sages: Write me for</b> future <b>generations</b> and canonize my book as part of the Bible. <b>They sent to her</b> that it is written: <b>“Have I not written for you three times”</b> (Proverbs 22:20), indicating that Israel’s battle with Amalek is to be mentioned <b>three times</b> in the Bible <b>and not four times?</b> Since it is already mentioned three times (Exodus 17:8–16; Deuteronomy 25:17–19; I Samuel 15), there is no need to add a fourth source.
The Sages did not accede to Esther’s request <b>until they found a verse written in the Torah: “Write this for a memorial in the book,</b> and rehearse it in the ears of Joshua: That I will utterly blot out the remembrance of Amalek from under the heavens” (Exodus 17:14). The Sages interpreted the verse: <b>“Write this,” that which is written</b> in the Torah <b>here</b> in Exodus, <b>and in Deuteronomy; “a memorial,” that which is written in the Prophets,</b> i.e., in I Samuel, on this matter; <b>“in the book,” that which is written in the Megilla.</b> The Megilla is the third mention of Amalek and not the fourth, as both mentions in the Torah pertaining to Amalek are considered one; therefore, Esther would be the third, not the fourth source.
The Gemara comments: This matter is <b>parallel</b> to a dispute between <b>the <i>tanna’im</i>,</b> as it was taught in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>“Write this,” that which is written here,</b> in the book of Exodus; <b>“a memorial,” that which is written in Deuteronomy; “in the book,” that which is written in the Prophets;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehoshua. Rabbi Elazar HaModa’i</b> disagrees and <b>says: “Write this,” that which is written</b> in the Torah <b>here</b> in Exodus, <b>and in Deuteronomy; “a memorial,” that which is written in the Prophets</b> on this matter; <b>“in the book,” that which is written in the Megilla.</b> Here too, the <i>tanna’im</i> disagreed whether or not the book of Esther has the same force and sanctity as that of the canonized books of the Bible.
<b>Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Shmuel said:</b> The book of <b>Esther does not render the hands ritually impure.</b> Although the Sages issued a decree that sacred scrolls render hands ritually impure, the book of Esther was not accorded the sanctity of sacred scrolls.
The Gemara asks: <b>Is this to say that Shmuel maintains</b> that the book of <b>Esther was not stated with</b> the inspiration of <b>the Divine Spirit? But didn’t Shmuel</b> himself <b>say</b> elsewhere that the book of <b>Esther was stated with</b> the inspiration of <b>the Divine Spirit?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>It was stated</b> with the Divine Spirit that it is <b>to be read</b> in public; <b>however, it was not stated</b> that it is <b>to be written.</b> Therefore, the text was not accorded the sanctity of sacred scrolls.
<b>The Gemara raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i>. <b>Rabbi Meir says:</b> The book of <b>Ecclesiastes does not render the hands ritually impure,</b> as it was not accorded the sanctity of sacred scrolls; <b>however, there is a dispute with regard to</b> whether or not <b>the Song of Songs</b> renders the hands impure. <b>Rabbi Yosei says: The Song of Songs renders the hands ritually impure, but there is a dispute with regard to</b> the book of <b>Ecclesiastes. Rabbi Shimon says:</b> The ruling with regard to <b>Ecclesiastes is among the leniencies of Beit Shammai and among the stringencies of Beit Hillel,</b> as according to Beit Hillel it renders the hands impure and according to Beit Shammai it does not. <b>However,</b> everyone agrees that the books of <b>Ruth, and the Song of Songs, and Esther render the hands ritually impure,</b> contrary to the opinion of Shmuel. The Gemara answers: It was Shmuel <b>who stated</b> his opinion <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehoshua</b> cited earlier that the book of Esther was not accorded the sanctity of sacred scrolls.
<b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Menasya says:</b> The book of <b>Ecclesiastes does not render the hands ritually impure because it is the wisdom of Solomon,</b> and not divinely inspired. <b>They said to him:</b> It was certainly divinely inspired and that is the reason that the book of Ecclesiastes was added to the canon; <b>as was it this alone that</b> Solomon <b>said? Wasn’t it already stated: “And he spoke three thousand proverbs,</b> and his poems were a thousand and five” (I Kings 5:12)? Solomon spoke many proverbs, but only a portion of them were canonized in the Bible. Apparently, what is unique about those in Ecclesiastes is that they were divinely inspired. <b>And it says: “Add you not unto his words”</b> (Proverbs 30:6).
The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is added by the proof introduced with the phrase: <b>And it says?</b> Why wasn’t the first proof sufficient? The Gemara answers: <b>And if you would say</b> that in terms of what <b>he said, he said a great deal,</b> with regard to <b>which, if he</b> so <b>desired, it was written, and if he</b> so <b>desired, it was not written;</b> then that is why not all of his statements were preserved. Therefore, <b>come</b> and <b>hear: Add you not unto his words.</b> Apparently, the reason that it is prohibited to add to the proverbs is that the book of Ecclesiastes was divinely inspired.
<b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> The book of <b>Esther was said with</b> the inspiration of <b>the Divine Spirit, as it is stated: “And Haman thought in his heart”</b> (Esther 6:6). If the book of Esther was not divinely inspired, how was it known what Haman thought in his heart? <b>Rabbi Akiva says:</b> The book of <b>Esther was said with</b> the inspiration of <b>the Divine Spirit, as it is stated: “And Esther obtained favor in the sight of all those who looked upon her”</b> (Esther 2:15); this could have been known only through divine inspiration.
<b>Rabbi Meir says:</b> The book of <b>Esther was said with</b> the inspiration of <b>the Divine Spirit, as it is stated</b> with regard to the conspiracy of Bigtan and Teresh against Ahasuerus: <b>“And the thing became known to Mordecai”</b> (Esther 2:22). This too could have been known only through divine inspiration. <b>Rabbi Yosei ben Durmaskit says:</b> The book of <b>Esther was said with</b> the inspiration of <b>the Divine Spirit, as it is stated: “But they did not lay their hands on the plunder”</b> (Esther 9:15). The only way that could have been stated with certainty is through divine inspiration.
<b>Shmuel said: Had I been there</b> among the <i>tanna’im</i>, <b>I would have stated a matter that is superior to them all, as it is stated: “They confirmed, and took upon themselves”</b> (Esther 9:27), which was interpreted to mean: <b>They confirmed above</b> in heaven <b>what they took upon themselves below</b> on earth. Clearly, it is only through divine inspiration that this could have been ascertained.
<b>Rava said: There is a refutation for all</b> of these proofs, <b>except for</b> the proof cited by <b>Shmuel, for which there is no refutation.</b> The Gemara elaborates. That <b>which Rabbi Eliezer</b> said with regard to knowledge of what Haman was thinking in his heart can be refuted, as <b>it is</b> based on <b>logical reasoning</b> to conclude that this was his thinking. <b>There was no</b> other <b>person as important to the king as he</b> was; <b>and</b> the fact is <b>that when he elaborated extensively and said:</b> “Let the royal apparel be brought” (Esther 6:8), <b>he said</b> it <b>with himself in mind.</b>
That <b>which Rabbi Akiva</b> said with regard to the knowledge that Esther found favor in the eyes of all, <b>perhaps</b> it can be understood and refuted <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar, who said:</b> This <b>teaches that she appeared to each and every one as</b> one of <b>his nation,</b> and they expressed that sentiment aloud.
<b>And that which Rabbi Meir</b> said, i.e., that the divine inspiration of the book of Esther is clear from the fact that Mordecai exposed the conspiracy against Ahasuerus, <b>perhaps</b> this can be explained and refuted <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba, who said: Bigtan and Teresh were both</b> members of the <b>Tarsi</b> people and conversed in their own language. Mordecai, who was a member of the Sanhedrin and therefore fluent in many languages, understood what they were saying.
<b>And that which Rabbi Yosei ben Durmaskit</b> said with regard to the knowledge that no spoils were taken, <b>perhaps</b> this can be explained and refuted by the fact that <b>they dispatched messengers</b> who informed them of the situation. However, <b>with regard to Shmuel’s</b> proof from the fact that they confirmed above what they took upon themselves below, <b>there is certainly no refutation. Ravina said: This</b> explains the folk saying <b>that people say: One sharp pepper is better than a basketful of pumpkins,</b> as the quality of the pepper’s taste is more significant than the quantity of the pumpkins.
<b>Rav Yosef said:</b> Proof that the book of Esther was divinely inspired may be cited <b>from here: “And these days of Purim shall not cease from among the Jews”</b> (Esther 9:28), an assertion that could have been made only with divine inspiration. <b>Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak says:</b> Proof may be cited <b>from here,</b> at the end of that verse: <b>“Nor the memorial of them perish from their seed”</b> (Esther 9:28).
The mishna mentions: <b>And gifts</b> distributed <b>to the poor. Rav Yosef taught</b> a <i>baraita</i> that the verse states: <b>“And of sending portions one to another”</b> (Esther 9:22), indicating <b>two portions to one person.</b> The verse continues: <b>“And gifts to the poor”</b> (Esther 9:22), indicating <b>two gifts to two people.</b>
The Gemara relates that, on Purim, <b>Rabbi Yehuda Nesia sent to Rabbi Oshaya the leg of a third-</b>born <b>calf and a jug of wine.</b> Rabbi Oshaya <b>sent him</b> a message of gratitude:

Daf 7b

<b>You have fulfilled</b> two mitzvot <b>through us, our teacher:</b> The mitzva of: <b>“And sending portions one to another,”</b> and the mitzva of: <b>“And gifts to the poor,”</b> as Rabbi Oshaya was poor and this was a substantial gift.
The Gemara relates that <b>Rabba sent</b> Purim portions from the house of the Exilarch <b>to Marei bar Mar in the hands of Abaye,</b> who was his nephew and student. The Purim portions consisted of <b>a sack [<i>taska</i>] full of dates [<i>kashva</i>] and a cupful of roasted flour [<i>kimḥa de’avshuna</i>]. Abaye said to him: Now, Mari will say</b> the popular expression: Even <b>if a farmer becomes the king, the basket does not descend from his neck.</b> Rabba was named the head of the yeshiva in Pumbedita, and nevertheless, he continued to send very plain gifts, because he was impoverished.
Marei bar Mar <b>sent back to him a sack full of ginger and a cupful of long peppers [<i>pilpalta arikha</i>],</b> a much more expensive gift. <b>Abaye said</b> to him: <b>The master,</b> Rabba, <b>will now say: I sent him sweet</b> items <b>and he sent me pungent</b> ones.
In describing that same incident, <b>Abaye said: When I left</b> the <b>house of the master,</b> Rabba, to go to Marei bar Mar, <b>I was</b> already <b>satiated.</b> However, <b>when I arrived there</b> at Marei bar Mar’s house, <b>they served me sixty plates of sixty kinds of cooked dishes, and I ate sixty portions from</b> each of <b>them. The last dish was called pot roast, and</b> I was still so hungry that <b>I wanted to chew the plate afterward.</b>
And in continuation <b>Abaye said: This</b> explains the folk saying <b>that people say: The poor</b> man <b>is hungry and does not know</b> it, as Abaye was unaware how hungry he had been in his master’s house. <b>Alternatively,</b> there is another appropriate, popular expression: <b>Room</b> in the stomach <b>for sweets</b> can always be <b>found.</b>
The Gemara relates that <b>Abaye bar Avin and Rabbi Ḥanina bar Avin would exchange their meals with each other</b> to fulfill their obligation of sending portions on Purim.
<b>Rava said: A person is obligated to become intoxicated</b> with wine <b>on Purim until he</b> is so intoxicated <b>that he does not know</b> how to distinguish <b>between cursed is Haman and blessed is Mordecai.</b>
The Gemara relates that <b>Rabba and Rabbi Zeira prepared a Purim feast with each other,</b> and <b>they became intoxicated</b> to the point that <b>Rabba arose and slaughtered Rabbi Zeira. The next day,</b> when he became sober and realized what he had done, Rabba <b>asked</b> God <b>for mercy, and revived him. The next year,</b> Rabba <b>said to</b> Rabbi Zeira: <b>Let</b> the <b>Master come and let us prepare the Purim feast with each other. He said to him: Miracles do not happen each and every hour,</b> and I do not want to undergo that experience again.
<b>Rava said: A Purim feast that one ate at night did not fulfill his obligation. What is the reason? “Days of feasting and gladness”</b> (Esther 9:22) <b>is written,</b> i.e., days and not nights. The Gemara relates: <b>Rav Ashi was sitting before Rav Kahana</b> his teacher on Purim, and <b>it grew dark and the Sages</b> who usually came to study with him <b>did not come.</b> Rav Ashi <b>said to him: What is the reason that the Sages did not come</b> today? Rav Kahana answered: <b>Perhaps they are preoccupied with the Purim feast.</b> Rav Ashi <b>said to him: Wasn’t it possible</b> for them <b>to eat</b> the feast <b>at night</b> on Purim, instead of being derelict in their Torah study on Purim day? Rav Kahana <b>said to him: Didn’t the master learn that which Rava said: A Purim feast that one ate at night did not fulfill his obligation?</b> Rav Ashi <b>said to him: Did Rava say that?</b> Rav Kahana <b>said to him: Yes.</b> Rav Ashi then <b>learned it from him forty times until</b> he remembered it so well that <b>it seemed to him as if it were placed in his purse.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> The previous mishna concluded with the formula: The difference between…is only, thereby distinguishing between the <i>halakhot</i> in two different cases. The following <i>mishnayot</i> employ the same formula and distinguish between the <i>halakhot</i> in cases unrelated to Purim and the Megilla. The first is: <b>The difference between Festivals and Shabbat</b> with regard to the labor prohibited on those days <b>is only</b> in <b>preparing food alone.</b> It is permitted to cook and bake in order to prepare food on Festivals; however, on Shabbat it is prohibited.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara infers <b>that with regard to the matter of</b> actions that <b>facilitate preparation of food,</b> e.g., sharpening a knife for slaughter, <b>this,</b> Shabbat, <b>and that,</b> Festivals, <b>are equal,</b> in that actions that facilitate preparation of food are prohibited.
The Gemara comments: If so, <b>the mishna is not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>The difference between Festivals and Shabbat is only preparing food. Rabbi Yehuda permits even</b> actions that <b>facilitate preparation of food</b> on Festivals.
The Gemara elaborates. <b>What is the reason</b> for the opinion <b>of the first <i>tanna</i>?</b> It is as the <b>verse states:</b> “Except that which every person must eat, only that may be done for you” (Exodus 12:16). <b>“That”</b> is permitted, <b>and not</b> actions that <b>facilitate</b> it. <b>And Rabbi Yehuda says: “For you”</b> means <b>for you, for all your needs.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> for <b>the other,</b> first, <i>tanna</i> <b>too, isn’t it written: “For you”?</b> The Gemara answers: He infers: <b>For you, and not for gentiles; for you, and not for dogs.</b> It is forbidden to perform labors for the sake of gentiles, or for animals, even if it is to feed them.
The Gemara asks further: <b>And</b> for <b>the other</b> <i>tanna</i>, Rabbi Yehuda, <b>too, isn’t it written: “That,”</b> which is a restrictive term that limits the application of a particular <i>halakha</i>? The Gemara answers: <b>It is written: “That,”</b> which is restrictive, <b>and it is written: “For you,”</b> which is inclusive. Rabbi Yehuda resolves the conflict between the two: <b>Here,</b> the word: “That,” is referring <b>to</b> actions that <b>facilitate,</b> in <b>which it is possible to perform them on the Festival eve</b> but which are prohibited on the Festival; <b>there,</b> the phrase: “For you,” is referring <b>to</b> actions that <b>facilitate,</b> in <b>which it is impossible to perform them on the Festival eve</b> and which are permitted even on the Festival.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>The difference between Shabbat and Yom Kippur</b> with regard to the labor prohibited on those days <b>is only that</b> in <b>this</b> case, i.e., Shabbat, <b>its intentional</b> desecration is punishable <b>at the hand of Man,</b> as he is stoned by a court based on the testimony of witnesses who forewarned the transgressor; <b>and</b> in <b>that</b> case, i.e., Yom Kippur, <b>its intentional</b> desecration is punishable at the hand of God, <b>with <i>karet</i>.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara infers <b>that with regard to the matter of payment</b> of damages, both <b>this,</b> Shabbat, <b>and that,</b> Yom Kippur, <b>are equal</b> in that one is exempt in both cases. If one performs an action on Shabbat that entails both a prohibited labor and damage to another’s property, since his transgression is punishable by death, he is exempt from paying damages. Apparently, according to the mishna, the same <i>halakha</i> applies to Yom Kippur.
The Gemara asks: According to <b>whose</b> opinion is <b>the mishna</b> taught? The Gemara answers: <b>It is</b> according to the opinion of <b>Rabbi Neḥunya ben HaKana, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Neḥunya ben HaKana would render Yom Kippur like Shabbat with regard to payment</b> of damages. <b>Just as</b> in the case of one who intentionally desecrates <b>Shabbat he is liable to</b> receive the <b>death</b> penalty <b>and</b> is therefore <b>exempt from</b> the obligation of <b>payment</b> of damages caused while desecrating Shabbat, <b>so too,</b> in the case of one who intentionally desecrates <b>Yom Kippur, he is liable to</b> receive the <b>death</b> penalty <b>and</b> is therefore <b>exempt from</b> the obligation of <b>payment</b> of damages caused while desecrating Yom Kippur.
<b>We learned there</b> in a mishna (<i>Makkot</i> 23a): <b>All</b> those <b>liable to</b> receive <b><i>karet</i> who were flogged</b> in court <b>were exempted from their <i>karet</i>,</b> which is imposed by heaven. Most transgressors are liable to receive <i>karet</i> for violating prohibitions that are punishable by flogging. If they are flogged, they are exempt from <i>karet</i>, <b>as it is stated</b> with regard to one liable to receive lashes: <b>“Then your brother shall be dishonored before you”</b> (Deuteronomy 25:3), indicating that <b>once he was flogged he is like your brother,</b> and his sins have been pardoned; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Ḥananya ben Gamliel. Rabbi Yoḥanan said: Rabbi Ḥananya ben Gamliel’s colleagues disagree with him</b> on this issue.
<b>Rava said</b> that the Sages of <b>the school of Rav said: We learned: The difference between Yom Kippur and Shabbat is only that</b> in <b>this</b> case, Shabbat, <b>its intentional</b> desecration is punishable <b>at the hand of Man; and</b> in <b>that</b> case, Yom Kippur, <b>its intentional</b> desecration is punishable <b>with <i>karet</i>. And if</b> the statement of Rabbi Ḥananya ben Gamliel <b>is so,</b> in both <b>this</b> case, Shabbat, <b>and that</b> case, Yom Kippur, the punishment <b>is at the hand of Man.</b>
<b>Rav Naḥman said:</b> There is no proof from here that Rabbi Ḥananya ben Gamliel’s colleagues disagree with him, as in accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> mishna taught? <b>It is</b> according to the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak, who said: There are no lashes in</b> cases of <b>those liable</b> to receive <b><i>karet</i>, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak says:</b> All <b>those liable</b> to receive <b><i>karet</i></b> in cases of incest <b>were included in</b> the principle: “For whoever shall commit any of these abominations, even the persons that commit them shall be cut off from among their people” (Leviticus 18:29). <b>And why was <i>karet</i></b> administered to <b>one’s sister excluded</b> from this verse and mentioned independently (Leviticus 20:17)? It is <b>to sentence her to</b> the punishment of <b><i>karet</i> and not to</b> the punishment of <b>lashes.</b> This serves as a paradigm; wherever one is liable to receive <i>karet</i>, there are no lashes.
<b>Rav Ashi said: Even</b> if <b>you say</b> that the mishna is according to the opinion of <b>the Rabbis</b> who disagree with Rabbi Yitzḥak and hold that there are lashes even in cases where there is liability for <i>karet</i>, there is no proof that Rabbi Ḥananya ben Gamliel’s colleagues disagree with him. The mishna can be understood as follows: In <b>this</b> case, Shabbat, the <b>primary</b> punishment for <b>its intentional</b> desecration is <b>at the hand of Man; and</b> in <b>that</b> case, Yom Kippur, the <b>primary</b> punishment for <b>its intentional</b> desecration is <b>with <i>karet</i>.</b> If, however, he was flogged, he is exempt from <i>karet</i>.

Daf 8a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>The difference between one for whom benefit from another is forbidden by vow and one for whom benefit from</b> another’s <b>food is forbidden by vow is only</b> with regard to <b>stepping foot</b> on his property, <b>and</b> with regard to borrowing <b>utensils</b> from him <b>that one does not use</b> in the <b>preparation of food,</b> but for other purposes; as those two benefits are prohibited to the former, but permitted to the latter.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara infers <b>that with regard to the matter of utensils that one uses</b> in <b>preparation of food,</b> both <b>this,</b> one who vowed that any benefit is forbidden, <b>and that,</b> one who vowed that benefit from food is forbidden, <b>are equal.</b> It is prohibited for both to derive benefit from utensils used in the preparation of food.
The mishna stated that for one for whom benefit from another is forbidden by vow, <b>stepping foot</b> on the latter’s property is prohibited. The Gemara asks: What benefit is that? <b>Aren’t people not particular</b> about other people treading on their property? <b>Rava said:</b> In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> mishna taught? It is the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer, who said: Overlooking is prohibited</b> in the case <b>of one for whom benefit is forbidden by vow.</b> For one for whom benefit from another is forbidden by vow, benefit is forbidden even in matters with regard to which one is typically not particular and overlooks others’ use of his property, e.g., stepping foot on it.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>The difference between</b> animals consecrated to the Temple as <b>vow</b> offerings <b>and</b> animals consecrated as <b>gift</b> offerings <b>is only that</b> in the case of <b>vow</b> offerings, if they died or were lost before being sacrificed on the altar, one <b>is obligated in</b> the <b>responsibility</b> to replace <b>them, and</b> in the case of <b>gift</b> offerings, if they died or were lost, one <b>is not obligated in</b> the <b>responsibility</b> to replace <b>them.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara infers <b>that with regard to the matter of</b> the prohibition: <b>Do not be slack</b> to pay one’s pledges, both <b>this,</b> a vow offering, <b>and that,</b> a gift offering, <b>are equal.</b> If one delayed bringing either a vow offering or a gift offering, he violates the prohibition.
<b>We learned</b> in a mishna <b>there: Which is</b> the case of <b>a vow</b> offering? It is <b>one who says:</b> It is incumbent <b>upon me</b> to bring <b>a burnt-offering. Which is</b> the case of <b>a gift</b> offering? It is <b>one who says: This</b> animal is a <b>burnt-offering. And what is</b> the difference <b>between a vow</b> offering <b>and a gift</b> offering? With regard to <b>vow</b> offerings, if the animals <b>died or were stolen or were lost,</b> the one who vowed <b>is obligated in</b> the <b>responsibility</b> to replace <b>them,</b> as he undertook to bring a burnt-offering and he is not absolved of his obligation until he brings the offering. With regard to <b>gift</b> offerings, however, if the animals <b>died or were stolen or were lost,</b> the one who vowed <b>is not obligated in</b> the <b>responsibility</b> to replace <b>them.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived? The Gemara answers: It is <b>as the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to a burnt-offering, that the verse states: <b>“And it shall be accepted for him to make atonement upon him”</b> (Leviticus 1:4). <b>Rabbi Shimon says: That which is</b> incumbent <b>upon him,</b> i.e., which he accepted as a personal obligation, <b>he bears responsibility</b> to replace <b>it</b> if it died or was stolen; <b>however, that which is not</b> incumbent <b>upon him,</b> i.e., that which he did not accept as a personal obligation but which he designated as an offering, he <b>does not bear responsibility</b> to replace <b>it.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>From where may</b> that conclusion <b>be inferred</b> from the verse? <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak bar Avdimi said: Since he said</b> it is incumbent <b>upon me</b> to bring a burnt-offering, he is <b>considered as one who bears</b> it <b>upon his shoulders.</b> The expression: Upon me, indicates an assumption of responsibility to bring an offering.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>The difference between a <i>zav</i> who experiences two emissions</b> of a pus-like discharge from his penis <b>and one who experiences three</b> emissions <b>is only</b> that the <i>zav</i> who experienced three emissions is obligated to bring <b>an offering</b> after he recovers, in order to complete his purification process.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara infers <b>that with regard to the matter of</b> transmitting ritual impurity to a surface designated for <b>lying and</b> a surface designated for <b>sitting, and</b> similarly with regard to <b>the counting of seven</b> days clean of emissions so that he may immerse in a ritual bath as part of the purification process, both <b>this,</b> i.e., one who experienced two emissions, <b>and that,</b> one who experienced three emissions, <b>are equal.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived? The Gemara answers: It is <b>as the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Simai says: The verse enumerated two</b> emissions: “When any man has an emission out of his flesh, due to his emission he is impure” (Leviticus 15:2), <b>and it called</b> the <i>zav</i> <b>impure.</b> Another verse enumerated <b>three</b> emissions: “And this shall be his impurity in his emission: Whether his flesh runs with his emission, or his flesh be stopped from his emission, it is his impurity” (Leviticus 15:3), <b>and it</b> too <b>called him impure. How so?</b> If he is impure after two emissions, for what purpose does the Torah mention three? It is to teach: <b>Two</b> emissions <b>to</b> establish <b>impurity and three to</b> render him liable to bring <b>an offering.</b>
The Gemara raises an alternative. <b>And say</b> instead: <b>Two</b> emissions <b>to</b> establish <b>impurity but not to</b> render him liable to bring <b>an offering; three</b> emissions <b>to</b> render him liable to bring <b>an offering, but not to</b> establish <b>impurity.</b> The Gemara rejects this: That is impossible, as <b>you</b> can <b>say</b> that <b>until he experienced three</b> emissions, <b>he</b> already <b>experienced two,</b> and therefore he is impure in the case of three emissions as well.
The Gemara raises a different alternative. <b>And say</b> instead: <b>Two</b> emissions <b>to</b> render him liable to bring <b>an offering, but not to</b> establish <b>impurity; three</b> emissions <b>to</b> establish <b>impurity as well.</b> The Gemara answers that this suggestion <b>cannot enter your mind, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that the verse states: <b>“And the priest shall make atonement for him before the Lord from his emission”</b> (Leviticus 15:15). The preposition “from” that precedes the words “his emissions” indicates that <b>some</b> with the status of a <b><i>zav</i> bring an offering and some</b> with the status of a <b><i>zav</i> do not bring an offering. How so?</b> If <b>he experienced three</b> emissions, <b>he brings</b> an offering; if he experienced <b>two</b> emissions, <b>he does not bring</b> an offering.
The <i>baraita</i> suggests: <b>Or</b> perhaps, <b>it</b> means <b>nothing other</b> than that if <b>one experienced two</b> emissions, <b>he brings</b> an offering; if <b>he experienced three</b> emissions, <b>he does not bring</b> an offering. The <i>baraita</i> rejects this: That is impossible, as <b>you</b> can <b>say</b> that <b>until he experienced three</b> emissions, <b>he</b> already <b>experienced two,</b> and therefore he is obligated to bring an offering in the case of three emissions as well.
The Gemara comments: <b>It was necessary</b> to cite the proof <b>of Rabbi Simai</b> based on the number of mentions of the word emissions in the two verses, <b>and it was necessary</b> to cite the proof from the words: <b>From his emission. As if</b> the difference between two and three emissions was derived <b>from</b> the statement <b>of Rabbi Simai, I would have said in accordance with our question:</b> Perhaps one who experiences two emissions brings an offering, and one who experiences three emissions is impure and brings an offering. Therefore, <b>it teaches us: From his emission. And if</b> the difference between two and three emissions was derived from the term: <b>From his emission, I would not have known how many emissions</b> render him liable to bring an offering, only that some with the status of a <i>zav</i> are not required to bring an offering. Therefore, <b>it teaches us</b> the proof cited <b>by Rabbi Simai.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And now that you said</b> that the term: <b>From his emission,</b> is exclusionary and comes <b>for derivation, what do you derive from</b> the verse: <b>“And when the <i>zav</i> is cleansed from his emission”</b> (Leviticus 15:13)?
The Gemara answers: <b>That</b> verse <b>is needed to</b> derive <b>that which was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>. It is written: <b>“And when the <i>zav</i> is cleansed</b> from his emission, then he shall count to himself seven days for his purification” (Leviticus 15:13), <b>when</b> his emissions <b>cease.</b> The <i>baraita</i> infers from the term: <b>From his emission,</b> that he needs to be cleansed only <b>from his emission, but not from his emission and his leprosy.</b> If one was both a <i>zav</i> and also had leprosy, he need not wait until he is asymptomatic of his leprosy before counting seven clean days. Rather, he counts seven clean days, and after the leprosy symptoms cease, he immerses for both impurities. <b>“From his emission, then he shall count”: This teaches concerning a <i>zav</i> who experienced two emissions, that he</b> too <b>requires a count of seven</b> clean days.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>But</b> in order to derive that <i>halakha</i>, the verse is unnecessary, as <b>isn’t it</b> only <b>logical?</b> If a <i>zav</i> who experienced two emissions <b>renders</b> a surface designated for <b>lying and</b> a surface designated for <b>sitting ritually impure</b> and all strictures of a <i>zav</i> apply to him, <b>wouldn’t he require a count of seven</b> clean days to become purified?

Daf 8b

<b>A woman who observes a</b> clean <b>day for</b> one <b>day</b> or two days that she experiences a discharge <b>will prove</b> that this is not the case. This refers to a woman who experienced one or two days of bleeding not during her menstrual period and is required to wait one day without any further discharge of blood before immersion in a ritual bath. This is significant <b>because she renders</b> a surface designated for <b>lying and</b> a surface designated for <b>sitting ritually impure, and</b> nevertheless <b>she does not require a count of seven</b> clean days to become purified.
<b>And you too should not</b> then <b>be surprised that this</b> <i>zav</i>, <b>although he renders</b> a surface designated for <b>lying and</b> a surface designated for <b>sitting ritually impure, he does not require a count of seven</b> clean days to become purified. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “From his emission, then he shall count,”</b> meaning that even <b>a partial <i>zav</i></b> is obligated in the mitzva of: <b>Then he shall count. This teaches concerning a <i>zav</i> who experienced two emissions, that he</b> too <b>requires a count of seven</b> clean days.
<b>Rav Pappa said to Abaye: What is different</b> about <b>this</b> verse that states: “<b>From his emission,” which</b> is interpreted to <b>include a <i>zav</i> who experienced two emissions</b> in the obligation to count seven clean days; <b>and what is different</b> about <b>that</b> verse that states: “<b>From his emission,” which</b> is interpreted to <b>exclude a <i>zav</i> who experienced two emissions</b> from the obligation to bring an offering? Why is the identical term interpreted once as inclusionary and once as exclusionary?
Abaye <b>said to him: If it enters your mind</b> to say that <b>this</b> instance of the term: “From his emission,” <b>comes to exclude</b> a <i>zav</i> who experienced two emissions from the obligation to count seven clean days, <b>let the verse remain silent</b> and omit the term, as there would have been no basis to include a <i>zav</i> who experienced two emissions in that <i>halakha</i>. <b>And if you would say</b> that this <b>can be inferred logically, a woman who observes a day for a day will prove</b> that there is no correlation between ritual impurity transmitted to a surface designated for lying and a surface designated for sitting, and the obligation to count seven clean days.
<b>And if you would say</b> that <b>this</b> term: From his emission, <b>is needed</b> to derive a different inclusion, i.e., that he counts seven days when he is clean <b>from his emission and not from his leprosy</b> and therefore it was necessary to write this term, that is not so. As <b>if</b> it were <b>so,</b> then <b>let the verse write: “And when the <i>zav</i> is cleansed” and let</b> the verse <b>remain silent</b> and omit the term, and it would have been clear that even one afflicted with leprosy counts seven clean days once he is cleansed from his emission. <b>Why</b> then <b>do I</b> need the term: <b>From his emission?</b> Rather, it must be understood as an inclusionary term that <b>teaches concerning a <i>zav</i> who experienced two emissions, that he</b> too <b>requires a count of seven</b> clean days.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>The difference between a quarantined leper,</b> i.e., one examined by a priest who found his symptoms to be inconclusive, and who must therefore remain in isolation for a period of up to two weeks waiting to see if conclusive symptoms develop; <b>and a confirmed leper,</b> i.e., one whose symptoms were conclusive and the priest declared him an absolute leper, <b>is only</b> with regard to <b>letting</b> the hair on one’s head grow <b>wild and rending</b> one’s garments. A confirmed leper is obligated to let the hair on his head grow wild and rend his garments; a quarantined leper is not.
<b>The difference between</b> a leper <b>purified from quarantine,</b> whose symptoms never became conclusive, <b>and</b> a leper <b>purified from</b> a state of <b>confirmed</b> leprosy <b>is only</b> with regard to <b>shaving</b> the hair on all his body <b>and</b> bringing <b>birds</b> as a purification offering, which are obligations incumbent only upon the confirmed leper.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara infers <b>that with regard to the matter of expulsion,</b> from all three camps in the encampment of the Israelites in the desert and from the walled cities in Eretz Yisrael, <b>and</b> the <b>ritual impurity</b> of a leper: Both <b>this,</b> the quarantined leper, <b>and that,</b> the confirmed leper, <b>are equal.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived? The Gemara answers: It is <b>as Rav Shmuel bar Yitzḥak taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>before Rav Huna.</b> It is written with regard to a leper who was purified from quarantine: <b>“The priest shall pronounce him clean: It is but a scab, and he shall wash his clothes and be purified [<i>vetaher</i>]”</b> (Leviticus 13:6). The word <i>vetaher</i> is not in the future tense, which would indicate that from that point he is purified; it is rather in the present tense, indicating <b>that at the outset,</b> even before the priest’s pronouncement, <b>he was pure</b> in the sense that he was exempt <b>from</b> the initial obligation of <b>letting</b> the hair on his head grow <b>wild and rending</b> his garments, as those obligations are incumbent exclusively upon the confirmed leper.
